# Super quick memory version

## 1. OOP
**Code using objects like real-world things.**

### Think:
- Car
- Student
- BankAccount

---

## 2. Class
**Blueprint / design / template**

### Think:
A class is the **plan** for making objects.

### Example:
`Car` class is the design of a car.

---

## 3. Object
**Real thing made from the class**

### Think:
If class is the plan, object is the actual car made from that plan.

---

## 4. Inheritance
**Child gets parent features**

### Think:
A child gets traits from parents.

Example:
- `Dog` gets features from `Animal`

---

## 5. Polymorphism
**Same name, different behavior**

### Think:
One action, different result.

Example:
- `sound()` in Dog = Bark
- `sound()` in Cat = Meow

---

## 6. Encapsulation
**Hide data inside class**

### Think:
Like a capsule — data is packed inside and not directly exposed.

---

## 7. Abstraction
**Show only needed part, hide the rest**

### Think:
You drive a car without knowing engine details.

---

## 8. Method overriding
**Child changes parent method**

### Think:
Parent has one method, child gives its own version.

---

## 9. Method overloading
**Same method name with different inputs**

### Important:
Python does **not support true method overloading**.

### Think:
In Python, we usually use:
- default arguments
- `*args`
- `**kwargs`

---

## 10. Instance method
**Works on object data**

### Think:
Uses `self`

---

## 11. Class method
**Works on class data**

### Think:
Uses `cls`

---

## 12. Static method
**Normal helper method, not tied to object or class**

### Think:
No `self`, no `cls`

---

## 13. `self`
**Current object**

### Think:
“this object”

---

## 14. `cls`
**Current class**

### Think:
“this class”

---

## 15. `__init__()`
**Constructor / setup method**

### Think:
Runs automatically when object is created.

It puts initial values into the object.

Imagine a **School**:

- **Class** = School plan
- **Object** = one actual school
- **Inheritance** = child school gets rules from main school
- **Polymorphism** = same activity, different school may do it differently
- **Encapsulation** = student records are protected
- **Abstraction** = you use school services without knowing all backend details
- **Overriding** = child class changes a parent rule
- **`self`** = this student
- **`cls`** = this school type
- **`__init__()`** = admission setup

## Copilot

Absolutely — here is a **quick memorization version** of all OOP terms with **easy definitions** and **tiny memory tricks**.

---

# OOP Quick Revision

## 1) OOP
**Definition:** A way of writing code using **objects**.  
**Easy meaning:** We model real-world things in code.

**Memory trick:**  
**OOP = Objects Oriented Programming**

---

## 2) Class
**Definition:** A **blueprint** for creating objects.  
**Easy meaning:** It tells what an object should have and do.

**Memory trick:**  
**Class = plan**

```python
class Car:
    pass
```

---

## 3) Object
**Definition:** A **real instance** of a class.  
**Easy meaning:** The actual thing made from the class.

**Memory trick:**  
**Object = actual thing**

```python
c = Car()
```

---

## 4) Inheritance
**Definition:** Child class gets features from parent class.  
**Easy meaning:** Reuse parent code.

**Memory trick:**  
**Inheritance = child gets parent’s property**

---

## 5) Polymorphism
**Definition:** Same method name, different behavior.  
**Easy meaning:** One name, many forms.

**Memory trick:**  
**Poly = many, morph = form**

---

## 6) Encapsulation
**Definition:** Hide data and control access.  
**Easy meaning:** Keep data safe inside class.

**Memory trick:**  
**Encapsulation = data hiding**

---

## 7) Abstraction
**Definition:** Show only important details, hide internal logic.  
**Easy meaning:** Use something without knowing full working.

**Memory trick:**  
**Abstraction = hide complexity**

---

## 8) Method Overriding
**Definition:** Child class gives its own version of a parent method.  
**Easy meaning:** Parent method is replaced in child.

**Memory trick:**  
**Override = replace parent method**

---

## 9) Method Overloading
**Definition:** Same method name with different parameters.  
**Easy meaning:** Python does not support true method overloading directly.

**Memory trick:**  
**Overloading = same name, different inputs**

---

## 10) Instance Method
**Definition:** A method that uses `self`.  
**Easy meaning:** Works with object data.

**Memory trick:**  
**Instance method = object method**

---

## 11) Class Method
**Definition:** A method that uses `cls`.  
**Easy meaning:** Works with class data.

**Memory trick:**  
**Class method = class-level method**

---

## 12) Static Method
**Definition:** A method that does not use `self` or `cls`.  
**Easy meaning:** Just a normal utility function inside class.

**Memory trick:**  
**Static = independent method**

---

## 13) `self`
**Definition:** Refers to the current object.  
**Easy meaning:** “this object”.

**Memory trick:**  
**self = current object**

---

## 14) `cls`
**Definition:** Refers to the current class.  
**Easy meaning:** “this class”.

**Memory trick:**  
**cls = current class**

---

## 15) `__init__()`
**Definition:** Constructor used to initialize object values.  
**Easy meaning:** Runs automatically when object is created.

**Memory trick:**  
**init = initialize**

---

# One-line super short revision

- **OOP** = code using objects
- **Class** = blueprint
- **Object** = real thing
- **Inheritance** = child gets parent features
- **Polymorphism** = same name, different behavior
- **Encapsulation** = hide data
- **Abstraction** = hide details
- **Overriding** = child changes parent method
- **Overloading** = same method, different inputs
- **Instance method** = uses `self`
- **Class method** = uses `cls`
- **Static method** = uses neither
- **self** = current object
- **cls** = current class
- **__init__()** = constructor

---

# Easy memory story
Think of a **school**:

- **Class** = school plan
- **Object** = actual student
- **Inheritance** = child gets traits from parent
- **Polymorphism** = same action, different behavior
- **Encapsulation** = student data kept safe
- **Abstraction** = you use school system without knowing all backend
- **Overriding** = child does a task differently
- **Instance method** = works for one student
- **Class method** = works for whole class
- **Static method** = general helper
- **self** = this student
- **cls** = this class
- **__init__()** = student details set at admission

Sure — here are the **exception handling questions** in **easy language**, with **examples and outputs** so you can revise quickly.

---

# 52) What is exception handling?

Exception handling is a way to handle errors safely so the program does not crash suddenly.

### Easy explanation
When something unexpected happens in code, like:
- dividing by zero
- file not found
- wrong type
- missing key

we use exception handling.

### Example
```python
try:
    x = 10 / 0
except ZeroDivisionError:
    print("Cannot divide by zero")
```

### Output
```python
Cannot divide by zero
```

### Why use it?
- avoids program crash
- gives user-friendly message
- helps debug problems

---

# 53) What is the difference between `try`, `except`, `else`, and `finally`?

## `try`
Code that may cause an error.

## `except`
Runs if error happens.

## `else`
Runs if no error happens.

## `finally`
Runs always, whether error happens or not.

### Example
```python
try:
    x = 10 / 2
except ZeroDivisionError:
    print("Error")
else:
    print("No error")
finally:
    print("Always runs")
```

### Output
```python
No error
Always runs
```

### Easy explanation
- `try` = test code
- `except` = handle error
- `else` = success block
- `finally` = cleanup block

---

# 54) What is raising an exception?

Raising an exception means **intentionally causing an error** in your code.

### Easy explanation
Sometimes we want to stop execution if input is invalid.

### Example
```python
age = 15

if age < 18:
    raise ValueError("Age must be 18 or above")
```

### Output
```python
ValueError: Age must be 18 or above
```

### Why use it?
To show that something is wrong on purpose.

---

# 55) What is the difference between `Exception` and specific exceptions?

## `Exception`
It is a general exception class.

## Specific exceptions
These are for particular errors like:
- `ZeroDivisionError`
- `ValueError`
- `TypeError`
- `KeyError`
- `FileNotFoundError`

### Example
```python
try:
    x = int("abc")
except Exception:
    print("Some error occurred")
```

### Output
```python
Some error occurred
```

### Specific example
```python
try:
    x = int("abc")
except ValueError:
    print("Invalid number")
```

### Output
```python
Invalid number
```

### Easy explanation
- `Exception` catches many errors
- specific exceptions catch only one kind of error

### Interview tip
Use specific exceptions when possible.

---

# 56) How do you create custom exceptions?

You can create your own exception by making a class that inherits from `Exception`.

### Example
```python
class InvalidAgeError(Exception):
    pass

age = 10

if age < 18:
    raise InvalidAgeError("Age should be 18 or above")
```

### Output
```python
InvalidAgeError: Age should be 18 or above
```

### Easy explanation
This is useful when built-in exceptions are not enough.

---

# Full example of exception handling

```python
try:
    num = int(input("Enter a number: "))
    result = 10 / num
except ValueError:
    print("Please enter a valid number")
except ZeroDivisionError:
    print("You cannot divide by zero")
else:
    print("Result:", result)
finally:
    print("Execution completed")
```

### Sample input
```python
2
```

### Output
```python
Result: 5.0
Execution completed
```

### Sample input
```python
0
```

### Output
```python
You cannot divide by zero
Execution completed
```

---

# Quick revision table

| Term | Meaning |
|---|---|
| `try` | code that may fail |
| `except` | handle the error |
| `else` | runs if no error |
| `finally` | runs always |
| raise | manually create an error |
| Exception | general error class |
| specific exception | error for one problem |
| custom exception | your own exception class |

---

# Easy memory trick

- **try** = try the code
- **except** = if error comes, handle it
- **else** = if no error, do this
- **finally** = always do this
- **raise** = create error yourself

# 10 Important Exception Handling Interview Questions

## 1) What is exception handling?
Exception handling is a way to handle runtime errors so the program does not crash suddenly.

---

## 2) Why do we use exception handling?
We use it to:
- avoid crashes
- show useful error messages
- make the program more reliable

---

## 3) What is the difference between `try` and `except`?
- `try` contains code that may cause an error
- `except` handles that error if it happens

---

## 4) What is the use of `finally`?
`finally` always runs, whether an error happens or not. It is commonly used for cleanup work like closing files.

---

## 5) What is the use of `else` in exception handling?
`else` runs only if no exception occurs in the `try` block.

---

## 6) What is raising an exception?
Raising an exception means manually creating an error using the `raise` keyword.

---

## 7) What is the difference between a general exception and a specific exception?
- General exception: `Exception`
- Specific exception: `ValueError`, `TypeError`, `ZeroDivisionError`, etc.

Specific exceptions are better because they catch only the exact error.

---

## 8) What is a custom exception?
A custom exception is your own exception class created by inheriting from `Exception`.

---

## 9) What happens if an exception is not handled?
The program stops and shows an error message.

---

## 10) Why should we catch specific exceptions instead of using only `except Exception`?
Because specific exceptions make debugging easier and avoid hiding other unexpected errors.

---

# Short code examples for revision

## Example 1: `try` and `except`
```python
try:
    num = 10 / 0
except ZeroDivisionError:
    print("Cannot divide by zero")
```

### Output
```python
Cannot divide by zero
```

---

## Example 2: `else` and `finally`
```python
try:
    num = 10 / 2
except ZeroDivisionError:
    print("Error")
else:
    print("No error")
finally:
    print("Always runs")
```

### Output
```python
No error
Always runs
```

---

## Example 3: custom exception
```python
class InvalidAgeError(Exception):
    pass

age = 15

if age < 18:
    raise InvalidAgeError("Age must be 18 or above")
```

### Output
```python
InvalidAgeError: Age must be 18 or above
```

Try solving these questions yourself.

## Part A: Basic practice
1. Write a program to divide two numbers and handle `ZeroDivisionError`.
2. Write a program to convert a string to integer and handle `ValueError`.
3. Write a program to access a list index and handle `IndexError`.
4. Write a program to access a dictionary key and handle `KeyError`.
5. Write a program to open a file that may not exist and handle `FileNotFoundError`.

---

## Part B: Intermediate practice
6. Write a program using `try`, `except`, `else`, and `finally`.
7. Write a program that takes age as input and raises an exception if age is less than 18.
8. Write a program that handles multiple exceptions in one `try` block.
9. Write a program that uses `finally` to print a cleanup message.
10. Write a program that creates and uses a custom exception.

---

## Part C: Challenge tasks

12. Write a program that opens a file, reads it, and closes it using exception handling.
13. Write a program that handles invalid login input using custom exception.
14. Write a program that checks if salary input is numeric and positive.
15. Write a program that catches only specific exceptions and not general ones.

---

# Sample answer for one assignment question

## Q: Divide two numbers and handle zero division
```python
try:
    a = int(input("Enter first number: "))
    b = int(input("Enter second number: "))
    result = a / b
    print("Result:", result)
except ZeroDivisionError:
    print("Cannot divide by zero")
except ValueError:
    print("Please enter valid numbers")
finally:
    print("Program finished")
```

# 56) How do you create custom exceptions?

A custom exception is your own error type.

## Easy explanation
Python already gives many errors, but sometimes we want our **own message** for a specific problem.

### How to create one
Make a class that inherits from `Exception`.

### Example
```python
class InvalidAgeError(Exception):
    pass

age = 15

if age < 18:
    raise InvalidAgeError("Age must be 18 or above")
```

### Output
```python
InvalidAgeError: Age must be 18 or above
```

---

## Another example with `try-except`
```python
class InvalidMarksError(Exception):
    pass

marks = 120

try:
    if marks > 100:
        raise InvalidMarksError("Marks cannot be greater than 100")
except InvalidMarksError as e:
    print(e)
```

### Output
```python
Marks cannot be greater than 100
```

---

# 57) What is file handling?

File handling means working with files in Python.

You can:
- open a file
- read data
- write data
- append data
- close file

### Easy explanation
It helps store data permanently in a file instead of only in memory.

### Example
```python
f = open("demo.txt", "w")
f.write("Hello Python")
f.close()
```

---

# 58) What are file modes in Python?

File modes tell Python **how to open a file**.

## Common file modes

| Mode | Meaning |
|---|---|
| `r` | read |
| `w` | write |
| `a` | append |
| `x` | create new file |
| `rb` | read binary |
| `wb` | write binary |
| `ab` | append binary |
| `r+` | read and write |
| `w+` | write and read |
| `a+` | append and read |

---

## Example
```python
f = open("demo.txt", "r")
```

This opens the file in **read mode**.

---

# 59) What is the difference between read mode and write mode?

## Read mode (`r`)
Used to **read existing content** from a file.

### Example
```python
f = open("demo.txt", "r")
print(f.read())
f.close()
```

### Output
```python
Hello Python
```

---

## Write mode (`w`)
Used to **write new content** to a file.

### Example
```python
f = open("demo.txt", "w")
f.write("New content")
f.close()
```

### Easy explanation
- `r` = take data out
- `w` = put data in

### Important note
`w` mode **overwrites** existing file content.

---

# 60) What is the use of `with` statement in file handling?

`with` is used to open a file safely.

### Easy explanation
It automatically closes the file after work is done.

### Example
```python
with open("demo.txt", "r") as f:
    data = f.read()
    print(data)
```

### Output
```python
Hello Python
```

### Why useful?
- no need to call `close()`
- safer
- cleaner code

---

# 61) Why should files be closed?

Files should be closed because:
- it saves system resources
- it ensures data is written properly
- it prevents memory/resource leaks

### Example
```python
f = open("demo.txt", "r")
data = f.read()
f.close()
```

### Easy explanation
If file is not closed, it may remain open in memory and cause problems.

### Best practice
Use `with`, because it closes automatically.

---

# 62) What is the difference between text and binary files?

## Text files
Contain human-readable data.

Examples:
- `.txt`
- `.csv`
- `.py`
- `.json`

### Example content
```python
Hello Python
```

---

## Binary files
Contain data in binary form, not directly readable by humans.

Examples:
- images
- audio
- video
- pdf
- executable files

### Example
Binary files are read with modes like `rb` and `wb`.

---

## Difference table

| Feature | Text File | Binary File |
|---|---|---|
| Readable by humans | Yes | No |
| Mode | `r`, `w`, `a` | `rb`, `wb`, `ab` |
| Example | `.txt`, `.csv` | `.jpg`, `.png`, `.mp3` |

---

# Full file handling example

## Write to file
```python
with open("demo.txt", "w") as f:
    f.write("Hello Python")
```

## Read from file
```python
with open("demo.txt", "r") as f:
    print(f.read())
```

### Output
```python
Hello Python
```

---

# Super quick revision

- **File handling** = working with files
- **File modes** = ways to open files
- **`r`** = read
- **`w`** = write
- **`a`** = append
- **`with`** = auto-close file
- **close file** = free resources
- **text file** = readable by humans
- **binary file** = not directly readable

---

# Easy memory trick

- `r` = **read**
- `w` = **write**
- `a` = **add at end**
- `with` = **safe open and close**
- text = **human readable**
- binary = **machine readable**

Sure — here are **modules and packages** in **easy language**, with **examples** and **quick revision points**.

---

# 63) What is a module?

A module is a **Python file** that contains code like:
- functions
- variables
- classes

### Easy explanation
A module is just one `.py` file you can reuse in another file.

### Example
Suppose you have a file called `mymodule.py`

```python
def greet():
    print("Hello from module")
```

Then in another file:

```python
import mymodule
mymodule.greet()
```

### Output
```python
Hello from module
```

---

# 64) What is a package?

A package is a **folder containing multiple modules**.

### Easy explanation
If module is one file, package is a collection of related files inside a folder.

### Example structure
```python
my_package/
    __init__.py
    math_utils.py
    string_utils.py
```

### Easy explanation
A package helps organize code better.

---

# 65) What is the difference between module and package?

| Feature | Module | Package |
|---|---|---|
| Meaning | single `.py` file | folder of modules |
| Example | `math_utils.py` | `my_package/` |
| Purpose | reusable code file | organize many modules |

### Simple explanation
- module = one file
- package = folder containing many files

---

# 66) What is `__init__.py`?

`__init__.py` is a special file inside a package folder.

### Easy explanation
It tells Python that the folder should be treated as a package.

### Example
```python
my_package/
    __init__.py
    math_utils.py
```

### Note
In modern Python, sometimes `__init__.py` can be empty, but it is still commonly used.

### Why is it useful?
- marks directory as package
- can run initialization code
- can define what gets imported from package

---

# 67) What is the difference between `import x` and `from x import y`?

## `import x`
Imports the whole module.

### Example
```python
import math
print(math.sqrt(16))
```

### Output
```python
4.0
```

You must use:
```python
math.sqrt()
```

---

## `from x import y`
Imports only the specific function/class/variable.

### Example
```python
from math import sqrt
print(sqrt(16))
```

### Output
```python
4.0
```

You can use:
```python
sqrt()
```

---

## Difference table

| Syntax | Meaning | Usage |
|---|---|---|
| `import x` | imports full module | `x.func()` |
| `from x import y` | imports only `y` | `y()` |

### Easy explanation
- `import x` = bring whole file
- `from x import y` = bring only one thing

---

# 68) What is `sys.path`?

`sys.path` is a list of directories where Python looks for modules to import.

### Easy explanation
When you write `import something`, Python checks folders in `sys.path` to find that module.

### Example
```python
import sys
print(sys.path)
```

### Output
It prints a list of folder paths.

### Easy explanation
Python searches these paths when importing modules.

---

# Real-life example of module use

## File: `calculator.py`
```python
def add(a, b):
    return a + b
```

## File: `main.py`
```python
import calculator

print(calculator.add(2, 3))
```

### Output
```python
5
```

---

# Quick revision table

| Topic | Easy meaning |
|---|---|
| Module | one Python file |
| Package | folder of modules |
| `__init__.py` | marks folder as package |
| `import x` | import whole module |
| `from x import y` | import only one item |
| `sys.path` | places where Python looks for modules |

---

# Easy memory trick

- **module** = one file
- **package** = many files in a folder
- **`__init__.py`** = package marker
- **`import x`** = whole box
- **`from x import y`** = one item from box
- **`sys.path`** = search path for imports

# 10 Module/Package Interview Questions

## 1) What is a module in Python?
A module is a single Python file that contains code such as functions, classes, or variables.

---

## 2) What is a package in Python?
A package is a folder that contains multiple Python modules.

---

## 3) What is the difference between a module and a package?
A module is one `.py` file, while a package is a folder containing multiple modules.

---

## 4) Why do we use modules?
We use modules to reuse code and keep programs organized.

---

## 5) Why do we use packages?
We use packages to organize related modules in a structured way.

---

It tells Python that a folder should be treated as a package.

---

`import x` imports the whole module, while `from x import y` imports only a specific part from it.

`sys.path` is a list of directories where Python searches for modules to import.

---

## 9) Can we import one module inside another module?
Yes, one module can import another module.

---

## 10) Why is package structure useful?
It helps keep code clean, reusable, and easier to maintain.

## @brehman_uhg

. Memory / performance
69.	What is shallow copy?

72.	What is garbage collection?

74.	Why is Python sometimes slow?

## Copilot

Sure — here are the **memory/performance questions** in **easy language**, with quick examples and revision points.

---

# 69) What is shallow copy?

A shallow copy creates a new outer object, but inner nested objects are still shared.

### Easy explanation
It copies only the top level.

### Example
```python
import copy

a = [[1, 2], [3, 4]]
b = copy.copy(a)

b[0][0] = 99

print(a)
print(b)
```

### Output
```python
[[99, 2], [3, 4]]
[[99, 2], [3, 4]]
```

### Easy memory trick
**Shallow = surface copy only**

---

# 70) What is deep copy?

A deep copy creates a full independent copy of the object and all nested objects.

### Easy explanation
It copies everything completely.

### Example
```python
import copy

a = [[1, 2], [3, 4]]
b = copy.deepcopy(a)

b[0][0] = 99

print(a)
print(b)
```

### Output
```python
[[1, 2], [3, 4]]
[[99, 2], [3, 4]]
```

### Easy memory trick
**Deep = full copy**

---

# 71) How does Python manage memory?

Python manages memory automatically for you.

### Easy explanation
Python:
- creates objects when needed
- stores them in memory
- removes unused objects automatically

### Main ideas
- **reference counting**
- **garbage collection**
- **memory allocator**

### Simple example
```python
a = 10
b = a
```

Here Python tracks how many variables are pointing to the same object.

### Easy explanation
You do not manually allocate and free memory in normal Python use.

---

# 72) What is garbage collection?

Garbage collection is the process of removing objects that are no longer used.

### Easy explanation
If an object has no references left, Python can delete it to free memory.

### Example
```python
a = [1, 2, 3]
del a
```

Now the list can be removed from memory if nothing else uses it.

### Easy memory trick
**Garbage collection = cleaning unused memory**

---

# 73) What is the GIL?

GIL stands for **Global Interpreter Lock**.

### Easy explanation
In CPython, only one thread can execute Python bytecode at a time.

### Why does it exist?
It helps protect Python’s memory management.

### Simple meaning
Even if you create many threads, only one thread runs Python code at a time in CPython.

### Important point
- helps with thread safety
- can limit performance in CPU-heavy multithreading

### Easy memory trick
**GIL = one Python thread at a time**

---

# 74) Why is Python sometimes slow?

Python can be slower than some other languages because:
- it is interpreted
- it uses dynamic typing
- GIL limits CPU-bound threading
- high-level features add convenience but can reduce speed

### Easy explanation
Python is designed to be easy to write and read, not always fastest.

### Example
A loop in Python may be slower than compiled languages like C++.

### Easy memory trick
**Python = easy and readable, but sometimes slower**

---

# 75) How do you optimize Python code?

You can optimize Python code by writing it more efficiently.

## Common ways:
- use built-in functions
- use list comprehensions
- avoid unnecessary loops
- use generators for large data
- use proper data structures
- cache repeated calculations
- use libraries like NumPy for heavy computation
- profile code to find slow parts

---

## Example: use list comprehension instead of loop
### Slower style
```python
squares = []
for i in range(5):
    squares.append(i * i)
```

### Better style
```python
squares = [i * i for i in range(5)]
```

---

## Example: use generator for large data
```python
def nums():
    for i in range(1000000):
        yield i
```

This saves memory because it gives values one by one.

---

## Easy optimization tips
- use `set` for fast lookups
- use `dict` when you need key-based access
- use `join()` for string concatenation
- use `enumerate()` when you need index and value
- avoid repeated computation inside loops

---

# Quick revision table

| Topic | Easy meaning |
|---|---|
| Shallow copy | copies only outer object |
| Deep copy | copies everything fully |
| Memory management | Python handles memory automatically |
| Garbage collection | removes unused objects |
| GIL | only one thread runs Python bytecode at a time |
| Python slow sometimes | interpreted + dynamic + GIL |
| Optimize code | use better structures, built-ins, generators, profiling |

---

# Easy interview answers

## What is shallow copy?
A copy of the outer object where inner objects are shared.

## What is deep copy?
A full independent copy of the object and nested objects.

## How does Python manage memory?
Python manages memory automatically using reference counting and garbage collection.

## What is garbage collection?
It is the process of freeing memory used by objects that are no longer needed.

## What is the GIL?
The Global Interpreter Lock allows only one thread to execute Python bytecode at a time in CPython.

## Why is Python sometimes slow?
Because it is interpreted, dynamically typed, and affected by the GIL.

## How do you optimize Python code?
Use efficient data structures, built-in functions, generators, and profile the code.

## @brehman_uhg

use enumerate() when you need index and value

## Copilot

Yes — **`enumerate()`** is used when you want **both index and value** in a loop.

---

# What is `enumerate()`?

`enumerate()` gives:
- the **position number** (index)
- the **item value**

### Example
```python
nums = [10, 20, 30]

for index, value in enumerate(nums):
    print(index, value)
```

### Output
```python
0 10
1 20
2 30
```

---

# Easy explanation
Normally, in a loop:

```python
for x in nums:
    print(x)
```

You only get the value.

But with `enumerate()`:

```python
for index, value in enumerate(nums):
    print(index, value)
```

You get:
- `index` = position
- `value` = item at that position

---

# Real-life example

Imagine a class list:

```python
students = ["Aman", "Ravi", "Sara"]

for i, name in enumerate(students):
    print(i, name)
```

### Output
```python
0 Aman
1 Ravi
2 Sara
```

---

# Start index from 1

By default, index starts from `0`.

If you want it to start from `1`:

```python
students = ["Aman", "Ravi", "Sara"]

for i, name in enumerate(students, start=1):
    print(i, name)
```

### Output
```python
1 Aman
2 Ravi
3 Sara
```

---

# Why use `enumerate()`?

It is useful when:
- you want item number
- you want to print serial number
- you want index and value together
- you want cleaner code than using `range(len(...))`

---

# Without `enumerate()`
```python
nums = [10, 20, 30]

for i in range(len(nums)):
    print(i, nums[i])
```

### Same output
```python
0 10
1 20
2 30
```

But `enumerate()` is cleaner and easier.

---

# Interview answer
You can say:

> `enumerate()` is used when we need both the index and value of items in a loop. It makes the code shorter and more readable.

---

## @brehman_uhg

what is generator

## Copilot

A **generator** is a special kind of function that gives values **one by one** instead of all at once.

It uses the keyword **`yield`**.

---

# Easy definition
A generator **generates** values only when needed.

---

# Example
```python
def my_gen():
    yield 1
    yield 2
    yield 3

g = my_gen()

print(next(g))
print(next(g))
print(next(g))
```

### Output
```python
1
2
3
```

---

# Easy explanation
Normal function with `return` gives one final result.

Generator with `yield`:
- gives one value
- pauses
- continues from the same place next time

---

# Real-life example
Think of a water tap:
- it gives water **drop by drop**
- not all water at once

A generator works like that.

---

# Why use generator?
- saves memory
- useful for large data
- gives items one at a time

---

# One-line interview answer
> A generator is a function that uses `yield` to produce values one at a time and pauses between each value.

---

If you want, I can also give you:
1. **generator vs iterator**
2. **generator vs return**
3. **real-life example of generator**
4. **10 generator interview questions**

## @brehman_uhg

generator vs iterator
generator vs return

## Copilot

Sure — here’s the **simple difference**.

---

# Generator vs Iterator

## Generator
A generator is a **simple way to create an iterator** using `yield`.

## Iterator
An iterator is an object that gives values one by one using `next()`.

---

## Main difference

| Feature | Iterator | Generator |
|---|---|---|
| What is it? | An object that returns items one by one | A special way to create an iterator |
| How created? | Using `iter()` or custom class | Using `yield` |
| Code length | Usually more code | Less code |
| Memory usage | Efficient | Very efficient |
| Simplicity | More complex | Simpler |

---

## Example of iterator
```python
nums = [1, 2, 3]
it = iter(nums)

print(next(it))
print(next(it))
print(next(it))
```

### Output
```python
1
2
3
```

---

## Example of generator
```python
def my_gen():
    yield 1
    yield 2
    yield 3

g = my_gen()

print(next(g))
print(next(g))
print(next(g))
```

### Output
```python
1
2
3
```

---

## Easy explanation
- **Iterator** = general object that can be looped with `next()`
- **Generator** = easy shortcut to make an iterator

---

# Generator vs Return

## `return`
- gives result once
- function ends immediately

### Example
```python
def fun():
    return 1
    return 2

print(fun())
```

### Output
```python
1
```

Only the first `return` runs.

---

## `yield`
- gives one value at a time
- function pauses and continues later

### Example
```python
def fun():
    yield 1
    yield 2
    yield 3

g = fun()

print(next(g))
print(next(g))
print(next(g))
```

### Output
```python
1
2
3
```

---

## Main difference

| Feature | return | yield |
|---|---|---|
| Output | one final value | multiple values one by one |
| Function behavior | ends function | pauses function |
| Memory | more memory if storing all values | saves memory |
| Use case | final result | sequence generation |

---

# Easy memory trick

- **return** = give and finish
- **yield** = give, pause, continue

---

# Interview-ready short answer

## Generator vs iterator
A generator is a simple way to create an iterator using `yield`. An iterator is any object that supports `next()` and returns values one by one.

## Generator vs return
`return` sends one final value and ends the function, while `yield` produces values one by one and pauses the function after each value.

1. **real-life example of generator**
2. **10 generator interview questions**
3. **difference between iterable, iterator, and generator**
4. **small practice assignment**

## @brehman_uhg

multhreading and python code 

## Copilot

Sure — here is **multithreading in Python** with **easy explanation** and **code example**.

---

# What is multithreading?

Multithreading means running **multiple threads** in the same program.

### Easy explanation
A thread is like a small worker inside a program.

If you have multiple threads, the program can do multiple tasks “at the same time” in a shared process.

---

# Why use multithreading?
Useful for tasks like:
- downloading files
- reading/writing files
- waiting for network responses
- background tasks

It is best for **I/O-bound tasks**, not heavy CPU work.

---

# Simple Python multithreading example

```python
import threading
import time

def task(name):
    for i in range(3):
        print(f"{name} running {i}")
        time.sleep(1)

t1 = threading.Thread(target=task, args=("Thread 1",))
t2 = threading.Thread(target=task, args=("Thread 2",))

t1.start()
t2.start()

t1.join()
t2.join()

print("Done")
```

---

# Example output
Output may vary because threads run together:

```python
Thread 1 running 0
Thread 2 running 0
Thread 1 running 1
Thread 2 running 1
Thread 1 running 2
Thread 2 running 2
Done
```

---

# Easy explanation of code
- `threading.Thread(...)` creates a thread
- `target=task` says which function the thread should run
- `args=("Thread 1",)` passes arguments to the function
- `start()` begins the thread
- `join()` waits for the thread to finish

---

# Another simple example

```python
import threading

def print_numbers():
    for i in range(5):
        print(i)

thread = threading.Thread(target=print_numbers)
thread.start()
thread.join()
```

---

# Important note
Python multithreading is limited by the **GIL** for CPU-heavy tasks, but it works well for I/O tasks.

---

# Interview answer
> Multithreading is a technique where multiple threads run inside the same program to perform tasks concurrently. In Python, it is useful mainly for I/O-bound tasks like file handling or network calls.

---

If you want, I can next give you:
1. **multithreading vs multiprocessing**
2. **10 multithreading interview questions**
3. **threading assignment**
4. **real-life multithreading example**

## @brehman_uhg

inheriteance polymophism and abstraction private protected class and object value

## Copilot

Here’s a quick, easy revision of all of them.

---

# 1) Inheritance
**Meaning:** One class gets features from another class.

### Example
```python
class Animal:
    def sound(self):
        print("Animal sound")

class Dog(Animal):
    pass

d = Dog()
d.sound()
```

### Output
```python
Animal sound
```

### Easy memory
**Child gets parent features**

---

# 2) Polymorphism
**Meaning:** Same method name, different behavior.

### Example
```python
class Dog:
    def sound(self):
        print("Bark")

class Cat:
    def sound(self):
        print("Meow")

for animal in [Dog(), Cat()]:
    animal.sound()
```

### Output
```python
Bark
Meow
```

### Easy memory
**One name, many forms**

---

# 3) Abstraction
**Meaning:** Hide unnecessary details and show only important things.

### Example
You drive a car without knowing engine details.

### In Python
Often done using abstract classes.

### Easy memory
**Hide complexity**

---

# 4) Private
**Meaning:** A variable/method meant to be used only inside the class.

### Example
```python
class Bank:
    def __init__(self):
        self.__balance = 1000

b = Bank()
```

`__balance` is private.

### Easy memory
**Private = inside class only**

---

# 5) Protected
**Meaning:** A variable/method meant for class and child class use.

### Example
```python
class Parent:
    def __init__(self):
        self._value = 10
```

`_value` is protected by convention.

### Easy memory
**Protected = class and child class**

---

# 6) Class
**Meaning:** A blueprint for creating objects.

### Example
```python
class Car:
    pass
```

### Easy memory
**Class = plan**

---

# 7) Object
**Meaning:** A real instance of a class.

### Example
```python
c = Car()
```

### Easy memory
**Object = actual thing**

---

# 8) Value
In Python, a **value** is the data stored in a variable.

### Example
```python
x = 10
name = "Aman"
```

Here:
- `10` is a value
- `"Aman"` is a value

### Easy memory
**Value = stored data**

---

# Very short revision table

| Topic | Easy meaning |
|---|---|
| Inheritance | child gets parent features |
| Polymorphism | same method, different behavior |
| Abstraction | hide details |
| Private | only inside class |
| Protected | class and child class |
| Class | blueprint |
| Object | real instance |
| Value | data stored in variable |

---

# One-line interview answers

## Inheritance
Child class gets properties and methods from parent class.

## Polymorphism
Same method name works differently in different classes.

## Abstraction
Hiding internal details and showing only necessary features.

## Private
Members used only inside the class.

## Protected
Members intended for class and child classes.

## Class
A blueprint for creating objects.

## Object
An instance of a class.

## Value
The actual data stored in a variable.

1. **private vs protected vs public**
2. **inheritance, polymorphism, abstraction with more examples**
3. **OOP full revision sheet**
4. **small assignment on these topics**

## @brehman_uhg

full code where all fucntion getting use with comments to explain me

## Copilot

Sure — here is a **single Python script** that shows **all the main OOP concepts together** with comments.

It includes:
- class and object
- constructor `__init__()`
- instance method
- class method
- static method
- inheritance
- method overriding
- encapsulation with private/protected-like attributes
- polymorphism
- abstraction idea
- `self` and `cls`

---

```python
# ==========================================
# OOP FULL EXAMPLE IN ONE FILE
# ==========================================

from abc import ABC, abstractmethod

# ------------------------------------------
# 1) CLASS
# A class is a blueprint for creating objects
# ------------------------------------------
class Person:
    # Class variable -> shared by all objects of this class
    species = "Human"

    # --------------------------------------
    # 2) CONSTRUCTOR (__init__)
    # This runs automatically when object is created
    # --------------------------------------
    def __init__(self, name, age):
        # Instance variables -> belong to each object
        self.name = name
        self.age = age

        # Protected-like variable (convention)
        self._country = "India"

        # Private variable (name mangling)
        self.__secret_code = 1234

    # --------------------------------------
    # 3) INSTANCE METHOD
    # Uses self, works with object data
    # --------------------------------------
    def show_info(self):
        print(f"Name: {self.name}, Age: {self.age}")

    # --------------------------------------
    # 4) INSTANCE METHOD USING PRIVATE DATA
    # Private variables cannot be accessed directly outside class
    # --------------------------------------
    def show_secret(self):
        print("Secret code:", self.__secret_code)

    # --------------------------------------
    # 5) CLASS METHOD
    # Uses cls, works with class data
    # --------------------------------------
    @classmethod
    def show_species(cls):
        print("Species:", cls.species)

    # --------------------------------------
    # 6) STATIC METHOD
    # Does not use self or cls
    # Used for general helper logic
    # --------------------------------------
    @staticmethod
    def is_adult(age):
        if age >= 18:
            print("Adult")
        else:
            print("Minor")

# ------------------------------------------
# 7) INHERITANCE
# Child class inherits from parent class
# ------------------------------------------
class Employee(Person):
    def __init__(self, name, age, salary):
        # Call parent constructor
        super().__init__(name, age)
        self.salary = salary

    # --------------------------------------
    # 8) METHOD OVERRIDING
    # Child class gives its own version
    # of parent's show_info()
    # --------------------------------------
    def show_info(self):
        print(f"Employee Name: {self.name}, Age: {self.age}, Salary: {self.salary}")

    # Extra method in child class
    def work(self):
        print(f"{self.name} is working")

# ------------------------------------------
# 9) POLYMORPHISM
# Same method name, different behavior
# ------------------------------------------
class Dog:
    def sound(self):
        print("Bark")

class Cat:
    def sound(self):
        print("Meow")

# ------------------------------------------
# 10) ABSTRACTION
# Abstract class can define common structure
# Child classes must implement abstract method
# ------------------------------------------
class Shape(ABC):
    @abstractmethod
    def area(self):
        pass

class Rectangle(Shape):
    def __init__(self, length, width):
        self.length = length
        self.width = width

    def area(self):
        return self.length * self.width

class Circle(Shape):
    def __init__(self, radius):
        self.radius = radius

    def area(self):
        return 3.14 * self.radius * self.radius


# ==========================================
# OBJECTS
# ==========================================
p1 = Person("Aman", 25)
e1 = Employee("Ravi", 30, 50000)

# ==========================================
# CLASS AND INSTANCE METHODS
# ==========================================
print("---- Person ----")
p1.show_info()
p1.show_secret()
Person.show_species()
Person.is_adult(p1.age)

print("\n---- Employee ----")
e1.show_info()      # overridden method
e1.show_secret()    # inherited method
e1.work()

# ==========================================
# POLYMORPHISM
# ==========================================
print("\n---- Polymorphism ----")
animals = [Dog(), Cat()]
for animal in animals:
    animal.sound()

# ==========================================
# ABSTRACTION
# ==========================================
print("\n---- Abstraction ----")
r = Rectangle(10, 5)
c = Circle(7)

print("Rectangle area:", r.area())
print("Circle area:", c.area())

# ==========================================
# PRIVATE / PROTECTED ACCESS DEMO
# ==========================================
print("\n---- Access Demo ----")
print("Protected-like variable:", p1._country)

# Direct access to private variable will fail:
# print(p1.__secret_code)   # Error

# Correct way through name mangling (not recommended for normal use)
print("Private variable using name mangling:", p1._Person__secret_code)
```

---

# What this code shows

## Class
`Person`, `Employee`, `Dog`, `Cat`, `Shape`, `Rectangle`, `Circle`

## Object
`p1`, `e1`, `r`, `c`

## Constructor
`__init__()`

## Instance method
`show_info()`, `show_secret()`, `work()`, `area()`

## Class method
`show_species()`

## Static method
`is_adult()`

## Inheritance
`Employee(Person)`

## Method overriding
`Employee.show_info()`

## Polymorphism
`Dog.sound()` and `Cat.sound()`

## Abstraction
`Shape` abstract class

## Encapsulation
`_country` protected-like
`__secret_code` private

---

# Expected output
```python
---- Person ----
Name: Aman, Age: 25
Secret code: 1234
Species: Human
Adult

---- Employee ----
Employee Name: Ravi, Age: 30, Salary: 50000
Secret code: 1234
Ravi is working

---- Polymorphism ----
Bark
Meow

---- Abstraction ----
Rectangle area: 50
Circle area: 153.86

---- Access Demo ----
Protected-like variable: India
Private variable using name mangling: 1234
```

---

# Very important notes

- `self` = current object
- `cls` = current class
- `__init__()` = constructor
- `_name` = protected-like by convention
- `__name` = private via name mangling
- abstract class cannot be directly instantiated

## @brehman_uhg

super keyword in python or this?

## Copilot

In Python, the keyword is **`super()`**, not **`this`**.

---

# `super()` in Python

`super()` is used to call the **parent class** method or constructor.

### Easy meaning
It helps a child class use code from the parent class.

---

## Example
```python
class Parent:
    def __init__(self):
        print("Parent constructor")

class Child(Parent):
    def __init__(self):
        super().__init__()
        print("Child constructor")

c = Child()
```

### Output
```python
Parent constructor
Child constructor
```

---

# Why use `super()`?
- to call parent constructor
- to reuse parent class code
- to avoid repeating code

---

# `this` vs `super()` in Python

## `this`
Python does **not** use `this` like Java or C++.

Python uses:
- `self` → current object
- `cls` → current class
- `super()` → parent class

---

# Simple memory trick
- **self** = this object
- **cls** = this class
- **super()** = parent class

---

# Another example
```python
class A:
    def show(self):
        print("A")

class B(A):
    def show(self):
        super().show()
        print("B")

b = B()
b.show()
```

### Output
```python
A
B
```

---

# Interview answer
You can say:

> Python uses `super()` to access parent class methods and constructors. Python does not use `this`; it uses `self` for the current object.

---

If you want, I can next give you:
1. **difference between `self`, `cls`, and `super()`**
2. **10 interview questions on `super()`**
3. **full inheritance example using `super()`**

## @brehman_uhg

super for accesing varaibale of parent what else can we access with super

## Copilot

`super()` is mainly used to access the **parent class methods and constructor**, not just variables.

---

# What can we access with `super()`?

## 1) Parent constructor
Most common use.

```python
class Parent:
    def __init__(self):
        print("Parent init")

class Child(Parent):
    def __init__(self):
        super().__init__()
        print("Child init")

c = Child()
```

### Output
```python
Parent init
Child init
```

---

## 2) Parent methods
You can call parent methods directly.

```python
class Parent:
    def show(self):
        print("Parent method")

class Child(Parent):
    def show(self):
        super().show()
        print("Child method")

c = Child()
c.show()
```

### Output
```python
Parent method
Child method
```

---

## 3) Parent class variables
You can access class variables through the parent class using `super()` in some cases.

### Example
```python
class Parent:
    x = 10

class Child(Parent):
    def show(self):
        print(super().x)

c = Child()
c.show()
```

### Output
```python
10
```

### Important note
This works for **class attributes**.  
For **instance variables** inside `__init__`, you usually access them using `self`, after calling `super().__init__()`.

---

# What `super()` cannot directly do well
- It is **not mainly used for instance variables**
- It is **not used to access child variables**
- It does **not replace `self`**

---

# Easy rule to remember

- **`super()`** → parent methods / parent constructor / parent class attributes
- **`self`** → current object values

---

# Example with variable in parent constructor
```python
class Parent:
    def __init__(self):
        self.name = "ParentName"

class Child(Parent):
    def __init__(self):
        super().__init__()
        print(self.name)

c = Child()
```

### Output
```python
ParentName
```

Here:
- `super().__init__()` sets the parent variable
- `self.name` accesses it

---

# Short interview answer
> `super()` is used to call parent class methods and constructors, and it can also access parent class attributes. For instance variables, we usually use `self` after calling the parent constructor.

---

If you want, I can give you:
1. **`super()` examples with variables, methods, and constructors**
2. **difference between `super()` and parent class name**
3. **full inheritance code using `super()`**

# 12) Set Operations

## What is a set?
A set is an **unordered collection of unique values**.

```python
s = {1, 2, 3, 3, 2}
print(s)
```

### Output
```python
{1, 2, 3}
```

---

## Common set operations

### 1. Union
Combines both sets.

```python
a = {1, 2, 3}
b = {3, 4, 5}

print(a.union(b))
```

### Output
```python
{1, 2, 3, 4, 5}
```

---

### 2. Intersection
Common items in both sets.

```python
a = {1, 2, 3}
b = {3, 4, 5}

print(a.intersection(b))
```

### Output
```python
{3}
```

---

### 3. Difference
Items in first set but not in second.

```python
a = {1, 2, 3}
b = {3, 4, 5}

print(a.difference(b))
```

### Output
```python
{1, 2}
```

---

### 4. Symmetric Difference
Items in either set, but not both.

```python
a = {1, 2, 3}
b = {3, 4, 5}

print(a.symmetric_difference(b))
```

### Output
```python
{1, 2, 4, 5}
```

---

### 5. Subset
Checks if one set is inside another.

```python
a = {1, 2}
b = {1, 2, 3, 4}

print(a.issubset(b))
```

### Output
```python
True
```

---

### 6. Superset
Checks if one set contains another.

```python
a = {1, 2, 3, 4}
b = {1, 2}

print(a.issuperset(b))
```

### Output
```python
True
```

---

### 7. Add and remove
```python
s = {1, 2, 3}
s.add(4)
s.remove(2)
print(s)
```

### Output
```python
{1, 3, 4}
```

---

## Exercise
1. Create two sets.
2. Print union.
3. Print intersection.
4. Print difference.
5. Print symmetric difference.
6. Check subset and superset.
7. Add one item and remove one item.

---

# 13) String Formatting

String formatting means putting values into a string nicely.

## 1. f-string
Best and most modern way.

```python
name = "Aman"
age = 25

print(f"My name is {name} and my age is {age}")
```

### Output
```python
My name is Aman and my age is 25
```

---

## 2. `format()`
```python
name = "Aman"
age = 25

print("My name is {} and my age is {}".format(name, age))
```

### Output
```python
My name is Aman and my age is 25
```

---

## 3. `%` formatting
Older style.

```python
name = "Aman"
age = 25

print("My name is %s and my age is %d" % (name, age))
```

### Output
```python
My name is Aman and my age is 25
```

---

## Formatting numbers
```python
pi = 3.14159
print(f"PI value: {pi:.2f}")
```

### Output
```python
PI value: 3.14
```

---

## Exercise
1. Print your name and age using f-string.
2. Print a price with 2 decimal places.
3. Use `format()` for one string.
4. Use `%` formatting once.

---

# 19) Import Variations

Python has different ways to import modules.

---

## 1. `import module`
```python
import math
print(math.sqrt(16))
```

### Output
```python
4.0
```

---

## 2. `import module as alias`
```python
import math as m
print(m.sqrt(25))
```

### Output
```python
5.0
```

---

## 3. `from module import name`
```python
from math import sqrt
print(sqrt(36))
```

### Output
```python
6.0
```

---

## 4. `from module import *`
Imports everything from a module.

```python
from math import *
print(sqrt(49))
```

### Output
```python
7.0
```

Use this carefully — it can make code confusing.

---

## Exercise
1. Import `math` normally.
2. Import `math` as alias.
3. Import only `sqrt`.
4. Compare `import x` vs `from x import y`.

---

# 20) GIL

GIL = **Global Interpreter Lock**

## Easy meaning
In CPython, only **one thread** can execute Python bytecode at a time.

---

## Why?
It protects Python memory management.

---

## Impact
- helps with safety
- limits CPU-bound multithreading performance

---

## Example idea
Even if you create multiple threads:

```python
import threading

def task():
    for i in range(3):
        print(i)

t1 = threading.Thread(target=task)
t2 = threading.Thread(target=task)

t1.start()
t2.start()
```

They run concurrently, but not truly in parallel for Python bytecode in CPython.

---

## Exercise
1. Read about GIL in CPython.
2. Write a multithreading example.
3. Observe thread behavior with sleep.

---

# 21) Garbage Collection

Garbage collection means removing unused objects from memory.

## Easy meaning
If an object is no longer needed, Python frees its memory.

---

## Example
```python
a = [1, 2, 3]
del a
```

Now `a` is removed.

---

## Python uses
- reference counting
- cyclic garbage collector

---

## Exercise
1. Create variables and delete them.
2. Use `gc` module to explore garbage collection.
3. Check object references.

---

# 22) Logging

Logging means saving messages about what your program is doing.

## Why use logging?
Instead of only `print()`, logging helps:
- debugging
- tracking errors
- recording events

---

## Basic example
```python
import logging

logging.basicConfig(level=logging.INFO)

logging.info("Program started")
logging.warning("This is a warning")
logging.error("This is an error")
```

### Output
```python
INFO:root:Program started
WARNING:root:This is a warning
ERROR:root:This is an error
```

---

## Logging levels
- DEBUG
- INFO
- WARNING
- ERROR
- CRITICAL

---

## Exercise
1. Set up logging.
2. Log info, warning, and error messages.
3. Log messages to a file.

# 23) Unit Testing

Unit testing means testing small pieces of code individually.

## Why use it?
To ensure functions work correctly.

---

## Example using `unittest`
### File: `calc.py`
```python
def add(a, b):
    return a + b
```

### File: `test_calc.py`
```python
import unittest
from calc import add

class TestCalc(unittest.TestCase):
    def test_add(self):
        self.assertEqual(add(2, 3), 5)

if __name__ == "__main__":
    unittest.main()
```

### Output
```python
.
----------------------------------------------------------------------
Ran 1 test in 0.000s

OK
```

---

## Exercise
1. Write a `subtract()` function.
2. Write a test for it.
3. Test multiple cases.
4. Try one failing test.

---

# 24) API Calls

API calls mean sending requests to a server and getting data back.

## Commonly used library
`requests`

---

## GET request example
```python
import requests

response = requests.get("https://jsonplaceholder.typicode.com/posts/1")
print(response.status_code)
print(response.json())
```

### Output
```python
200
{'userId': 1, 'id': 1, 'title': '...', 'body': '...'}
```

---

## POST request example
```python
import requests

data = {"name": "Aman", "age": 25}
response = requests.post("https://jsonplaceholder.typicode.com/posts", json=data)

print(response.status_code)
print(response.json())
```

---

## Exercise
1. Make a GET request.
2. Print status code.
3. Convert response to JSON.
4. Make a POST request with sample data.

---

# 25) JSON Handling

JSON = JavaScript Object Notation  
It is a common data format used in APIs.

---

## Python dictionary to JSON
```python
import json

data = {"name": "Aman", "age": 25}
json_str = json.dumps(data)

print(json_str)
```

### Output
```python
{"name": "Aman", "age": 25}
```

---

## JSON string to Python dictionary
```python
import json

json_str = '{"name": "Aman", "age": 25}'
data = json.loads(json_str)

print(data)
print(data["name"])
```

### Output
```python
{'name': 'Aman', 'age': 25}
Aman
```

---

## JSON file handling
### Write JSON
```python
import json

data = {"name": "Aman", "age": 25}

with open("data.json", "w") as f:
    json.dump(data, f)
```

### Read JSON
```python
import json

with open("data.json", "r") as f:
    data = json.load(f)
    print(data)
```

---

## Exercise
1. Convert dict to JSON string.
2. Convert JSON string to dict.
3. Save JSON to file.
4. Read JSON from file.

---

# 50 Advanced Python Interview Questions

## Python internals and memory
1. What is the difference between shallow copy and deep copy?
2. How does Python manage memory?
3. What is garbage collection in Python?
4. What is reference counting?
5. What is the GIL?
6. Why is Python sometimes slow?
7. How do you optimize Python code?
8. What is the difference between `is` and `==`?
9. What is interning in Python?
10. What is object mutability and immutability?

---

## OOP and class behavior
11. What is the difference between `self`, `cls`, and `super()`?
12. What is method resolution order (MRO)?
13. What is multiple inheritance and how does Python resolve it?
14. What is method overriding?
15. What is polymorphism?
16. What is abstraction?
17. What is encapsulation?
18. What are private, protected, and public members?
19. What are class methods, static methods, and instance methods?
20. What are decorators and how are they used with methods?

---

## Functions and advanced language features
21. What are `*args` and `**kwargs`?
22. What is a lambda function?

## @brehman_uhg

What is the difference between json.dump() and json.dumps()?
What is the difference between json.load() and json.loads()?

## Copilot

Here’s the simple difference:

---

# `json.dump()` vs `json.dumps()`

## `json.dump()`
Writes Python data **into a file** as JSON.

### Example
```python
import json

data = {"name": "Aman", "age": 25}

with open("data.json", "w") as f:
    json.dump(data, f)
```

### Meaning
- **dump = write to file**
- output goes into a file

---

## `json.dumps()`
Converts Python data **into a JSON string**.

### Example
```python
import json

data = {"name": "Aman", "age": 25}

json_string = json.dumps(data)
print(json_string)
```

### Output
```python
{"name": "Aman", "age": 25}
```

### Meaning
- **dumps = string**
- output is a text string, not a file

---

## Difference table

| Function | Output | Use |
|---|---|---|
| `json.dump()` | writes JSON to file | save data to file |
| `json.dumps()` | returns JSON string | convert to string |

---

# `json.load()` vs `json.loads()`

## `json.load()`
Reads JSON data **from a file** and converts it into Python object.

### Example
```python
import json

with open("data.json", "r") as f:
    data = json.load(f)

print(data)
```

### Meaning
- **load = read from file**

---

## `json.loads()`
Reads JSON data **from a string** and converts it into Python object.

### Example
```python
import json

json_string = '{"name": "Aman", "age": 25}'
data = json.loads(json_string)

print(data)
```

### Output
```python
{'name': 'Aman', 'age': 25}
```

### Meaning
- **loads = string**

---

## Difference table

| Function | Input | Use |
|---|---|---|
| `json.load()` | file object | read JSON from file |
| `json.loads()` | JSON string | read JSON from string |

---

# Easy memory trick

- **dump** = write to file
- **dumps** = string
- **load** = read from file
- **loads** = string

---

# One-line interview answer

> `json.dump()` writes Python data to a file as JSON, while `json.dumps()` converts Python data to a JSON string. Similarly, `json.load()` reads JSON from a file, while `json.loads()` reads JSON from a string.

---

1. **small code examples for all four together**
2. **10 JSON interview questions**
3. **practice assignment on JSON handling**

## @brehman_uhg

Python’s argument-passing model is neither “Pass by Value” nor “Pass by Reference” but it is “Pass by Object Reference”. 
Depending on the type of object you pass in the function, the function behaves differently. Immutable objects show “pass by value” whereas mutable objects show “pass by reference”.
You can check the difference between pass-by-value and pass-by-reference in the example below

## Copilot

Yes — that’s a **common interview explanation**, but it needs a **small correction**:

## Correct idea
Python is **pass by object reference**  
or more precisely **pass by assignment**.

That means:
- the function receives a **reference to the object**
- the function can **change the object itself** if it is mutable
- but it **cannot change the caller’s variable binding** in the same way as pass-by-reference in some other languages

---

# Important correction
This part is **not fully correct**:

> “Immutable objects show pass by value whereas mutable objects show pass by reference”

Better way to say it:

- **Immutable objects** may *look like* pass by value because they cannot be changed in place
- **Mutable objects** may *look like* pass by reference because changes inside the function affect the same object

But Python is **still not true pass-by-value or true pass-by-reference**.

---

# Example with immutable object

```python
def change_num(x):
    x = 20
    print("Inside function:", x)

num = 10
change_num(num)
print("Outside function:", num)
```

### Output
```python
Inside function: 20
Outside function: 10
```

### Why?
- `num` is immutable
- inside the function, `x` gets a new value
- outside value does not change

---

# Example with mutable object

```python
def change_list(lst):
    lst.append(4)
    print("Inside function:", lst)

numbers = [1, 2, 3]
change_list(numbers)
print("Outside function:", numbers)
```

### Output
```python
Inside function: [1, 2, 3, 4]
Outside function: [1, 2, 3, 4]
```

### Why?
- list is mutable
- function changes the same object
- outside value also changes

---

# Another important example: reassigning mutable object

```python
def change_list(lst):
    lst = [9, 9, 9]
    print("Inside function:", lst)

numbers = [1, 2, 3]
change_list(numbers)
print("Outside function:", numbers)
```

### Output
```python
Inside function: [9, 9, 9]
Outside function: [1, 2, 3]
```

### Why?
- `lst` now points to a new list inside the function
- original list outside is unchanged

---

# Best interview answer

> Python uses pass by object reference, also called pass by assignment. The function receives a reference to the object, and behavior depends on whether the object is mutable or immutable. Mutable objects can be modified in place, while immutable objects cannot.

---

# Easy memory trick
- **immutable** = cannot change in place
- **mutable** = can change in place
- Python passes **object references**, not raw values

---

If you want, I can give you:
1. **a very simple diagram explanation**
2. **interview-ready 2-line answer**
3. **5 practice questions on mutable vs immutable and passing arguments**

## @brehman_uhg

. What are Decorators?
Decorators are a powerful and flexible way to modify or extend the behavior of functions or methods, without changing their actual code. A decorator is essentially a function that takes another function as an argument and returns a new function with enhanced functionality.

Decorators are often used in scenarios such as logging, authentication and memorization, allowing us to add additional functionality to existing functions or methods in a clean, reusable way.

## Copilot

Yes — that definition is correct. Here’s a cleaner, easier version with code.

---

# What are decorators?

A **decorator** is a function that takes another function and **adds extra behavior** to it **without changing the original function code**.

---

# Easy meaning
Decorators are used when you want to:
- add logging
- check login/authentication
- measure time
- reuse common code
- cache results

---

# Simple decorator example

```python
def my_decorator(func):
    def wrapper():
        print("Before function call")
        func()
        print("After function call")
    return wrapper

@my_decorator
def say_hello():
    print("Hello!")

say_hello()
```

### Output
```python
Before function call
Hello!
After function call
```

---

# How it works
This:

```python
@my_decorator
def say_hello():
    print("Hello!")
```

is the same as:

```python
def say_hello():
    print("Hello!")

say_hello = my_decorator(say_hello)
```

---

# Decorator with arguments

```python
def my_decorator(func):
    def wrapper(name):
        print("Before function call")
        func(name)
        print("After function call")
    return wrapper

@my_decorator
def greet(name):
    print("Hello", name)

greet("Aman")
```

### Output
```python
Before function call
Hello Aman
After function call
```

---

# Why decorators are useful

They help you add common features to many functions:
- authentication
- validation
- timing
- debugging
- caching

without repeating code again and again.

---

# Real-life example
Imagine every function needs:
- start log
- end log

Instead of writing that in every function, use a decorator.

---

# Interview answer
> A decorator is a function that wraps another function to add extra behavior without modifying the original function. It is commonly used for logging, authentication, timing, and caching.

---

# Important note
You wrote **“memorization”** — in Python this is usually **“memoization”**.

- **memoization** = storing results of expensive function calls to avoid repeating work

---

If you want, I can next give you:
1. **10 decorator interview questions**
2. **decorator with arguments**
3. **class decorators**
4. **memoization example with decorator**

## @brehman_uhg

whar and why its here wrapper

## Copilot

`wrapper` is just the **inner function** inside a decorator.

It is called `wrapper` because it **wraps around** the original function and adds extra code before and after it.

---

# Why is `wrapper` used?

Because a decorator must:
1. take a function as input
2. run extra code
3. call the original function
4. return something

The inner function does that job, so we usually name it `wrapper`.

---

# Example

```python
def my_decorator(func):
    def wrapper():
        print("Before")
        func()
        print("After")
    return wrapper

@my_decorator
def hello():
    print("Hello")

hello()
```

### Output
```python
Before
Hello
After
```

---

# What is happening here?

- `my_decorator` is the decorator function
- `func` is the original function passed in
- `wrapper` is the function that wraps `func`
- `return wrapper` gives back the new modified function

---

# Simple reason
We cannot just write extra code directly around the function after it is defined, so we create a **new function inside the decorator**.

That inner function is usually called `wrapper`.

---

# Can it have another name?
Yes. `wrapper` is just a common name.

Example:

```python
def my_decorator(func):
    def inner():
        print("Before")
        func()
        print("After")
    return inner
```

Here `inner` works the same way.

---

# Easy memory trick
- decorator = outer function
- wrapper = inner function
- wrapper surrounds the original function

---

# Interview answer
> The wrapper is the inner function inside a decorator that adds extra behavior before and after calling the original function. It is called wrapper because it wraps the original function.

---

If you want, I can next give you:
1. **decorator with arguments**
2. **step-by-step flow of decorator execution**
3. **a diagram of decorator + wrapper**
4. **10 practice questions on decorators**

## @brehman_uhg

why do we use @ annoateion here

## Copilot

We use the `@` annotation because it is the **short, readable syntax** for applying a decorator.

---

# Why use `@`?

Instead of writing this:

```python
def hello():
    print("Hello")

hello = my_decorator(hello)
```

we can write:

```python
@my_decorator
def hello():
    print("Hello")
```

Both do the **same thing**.

---

# What does `@` do?

It tells Python:

> “Take this function and pass it into the decorator.”

So this:

```python
@my_decorator
def hello():
    print("Hello")
```

means:

```python
hello = my_decorator(hello)
```

---

# Why is it useful?

## 1) Cleaner code
It looks shorter and easier to read.

## 2) Better style
Decorators are commonly written with `@`.

## 3) Reusable
The same decorator can be applied to many functions easily.

---

# Example

```python
def my_decorator(func):
    def wrapper():
        print("Before")
        func()
        print("After")
    return wrapper

@my_decorator
def hello():
    print("Hello")

hello()
```

### Output
```python
Before
Hello
After
```

---

# Without `@`
```python
def my_decorator(func):
    def wrapper():
        print("Before")
        func()
        print("After")
    return wrapper

def hello():
    print("Hello")

hello = my_decorator(hello)
hello()
```

Same output.

---

# Easy memory trick
- `@decorator` = shortcut syntax
- it applies the decorator to the function below it

---

# Interview answer
> The `@` symbol is used as syntactic sugar to apply a decorator to a function in a clean and readable way. It is equivalent to writing `function = decorator(function)`.

---

1. **decorator flow step by step**
2. **how `@` works with arguments**
3. **class decorators**
4. **10 decorator interview questions**

## @brehman_uhg

interfaces and abstract class in python and full code also

## Copilot

In Python, **interfaces are usually represented using abstract base classes (ABCs)**. Python does not have a separate `interface` keyword like Java, but you can achieve the same behavior with `abc.ABC` and `@abstractmethod`.

---

# 1) Abstract class

An abstract class is a class that:
- cannot be created directly
- can contain abstract methods
- may also contain normal methods

### Meaning
It gives a common structure for child classes.

---

# 2) Interface in Python

Python does not have a built-in interface keyword, but an interface-like class is:
- a class with only abstract methods
- used to force child classes to implement those methods

---

# Difference between abstract class and interface-like class

| Feature | Abstract Class | Interface-like Class |
|---|---|---|
| Can have normal methods | Yes | Usually no |
| Can have variables | Yes | Usually minimal |
| Purpose | share code + force methods | force method implementation |
| Instantiable | No | No |

---

# Full code example

```python
from abc import ABC, abstractmethod

# -----------------------------------------
# Abstract class
# -----------------------------------------
class Animal(ABC):
    def __init__(self, name):
        self.name = name

    # Abstract method: child class must implement this
    @abstractmethod
    def sound(self):
        pass

    # Normal method: child class can use directly
    def sleep(self):
        print(f"{self.name} is sleeping")


# -----------------------------------------
# Interface-like abstract class
# Usually only abstract methods
# -----------------------------------------
class Flyable(ABC):
    @abstractmethod
    def fly(self):
        pass


class Swimmable(ABC):
    @abstractmethod
    def swim(self):
        pass


# -----------------------------------------
# Child classes
# -----------------------------------------
class Dog(Animal):
    def sound(self):
        print(f"{self.name} says Bark")


class Bird(Animal, Flyable):
    def sound(self):
        print(f"{self.name} says Chirp")

    def fly(self):
        print(f"{self.name} is flying")


class Duck(Animal, Flyable, Swimmable):
    def sound(self):
        print(f"{self.name} says Quack")

    def fly(self):
        print(f"{self.name} is flying")

    def swim(self):
        print(f"{self.name} is swimming")


# -----------------------------------------
# Objects
# -----------------------------------------
dog = Dog("Tommy")
bird = Bird("Tweety")
duck = Duck("Donald")

# -----------------------------------------
# Using methods
# -----------------------------------------
dog.sound()
dog.sleep()

bird.sound()
bird.fly()
bird.sleep()

duck.sound()
duck.fly()
duck.swim()
duck.sleep()
```

---

# Output

```python
Tommy says Bark
Tommy is sleeping
Tweety says Chirp
Tweety is flying
Tweety is sleeping
Donald says Quack
Donald is flying
Donald is swimming
Donald is sleeping
```

---

# What this code shows

## Abstract class
`Animal`
- has a normal method `sleep()`
- has an abstract method `sound()`

## Interface-like classes
`Flyable`, `Swimmable`
- only abstract methods
- force classes to implement them

## Child classes
- `Dog` implements `sound()`
- `Bird` implements `sound()` and `fly()`
- `Duck` implements `sound()`, `fly()`, and `swim()`

---

# Important notes

## You cannot create object of abstract class
```python
a = Animal("Test")   # Error
```

Because `Animal` has an abstract method.

---

## If child class does not implement abstract methods
Python will also prevent object creation.

Example:
```python
class Cat(Animal):
    pass

c = Cat("Kitty")   # Error
```

---

# Easy interview answer

## Abstract class
An abstract class is a class that cannot be instantiated directly and may contain abstract methods and normal methods.

## Interface in Python
Python does not have a separate interface keyword. Interfaces are usually implemented using abstract classes with only abstract methods.

---

# Easy memory trick
- **Abstract class** = blueprint with some shared code
- **Interface** = contract that says “you must implement these methods”

---

1. **difference between abstract class and interface in simple table**
2. **10 interview questions on abstract class and interface**
3. **same code with line-by-line explanation**
4. **practice assignment on abstraction/interface**

## @brehman_uhg

17. What are metaclasses in Python, and how do they differ from regular classes?
Metaclasses are classes of classes. They define how classes behave and are created. While regular classes create objects, metaclasses create classes. By using metaclasses, you can modify class definitions, enforce rules, or add functionality during class creation.

Example:

class Meta(type):
    def __new__(cls, name, bases, dct):
        print(f"Creating class {name}")
        return super().__new__(cls, name, bases, dct)

class MyClass(metaclass=Meta):
    pass
# Output: Creating class MyClass

## Copilot

Yes — that explanation is correct.

Here’s a cleaner version with simple code and the key difference.

---

# What are metaclasses in Python?

A **metaclass** is a **class that creates classes**.

- Regular class → creates **objects**
- Metaclass → creates **classes**

---

# Easy meaning
If a class is a blueprint for objects, then a metaclass is a blueprint for classes.

---

# Regular class vs metaclass

| Thing | Creates |
|---|---|
| Class | Objects |
| Metaclass | Classes |

---

# Example of regular class
```python
class Car:
    pass

c = Car()
```

Here:
- `Car` is a class
- `c` is an object

---

# Example of metaclass
```python
class Meta(type):
    def __new__(cls, name, bases, dct):
        print(f"Creating class {name}")
        return super().__new__(cls, name, bases, dct)

class MyClass(metaclass=Meta):
    pass
```

### Output
```python
Creating class MyClass
```

---

# What is happening?
When Python sees:

```python
class MyClass(metaclass=Meta):
```

it uses `Meta` to create `MyClass`.

So `Meta` controls class creation.

---

# Why use metaclasses?

Metaclasses are used when you want to:
- enforce rules on classes
- automatically add methods or attributes
- register classes
- control class creation
- validate class structure

---

# Simple example: add an attribute automatically

```python
class Meta(type):
    def __new__(cls, name, bases, dct):
        dct["created_by"] = "MetaClass"
        return super().__new__(cls, name, bases, dct)

class Demo(metaclass=Meta):
    pass

print(Demo.created_by)
```

### Output
```python
MetaClass
```

---

# Easy interview answer

> A metaclass is a class that creates other classes. Regular classes create objects, but metaclasses control how classes are created and can modify class behavior at creation time.

---

# Important note
Metaclasses are advanced and not needed in daily Python most of the time.  
They are useful in frameworks and advanced library design.

---

# Simple memory trick
- **class** → makes objects
- **metaclass** → makes classes

---

If you want, I can next give you:
1. **full metaclass code with comments**
2. **difference between class, object, and metaclass**
3. **10 metaclass interview questions**
4. **real-life use cases of metaclasses**
