# 4) AWS ECR

```bash
# ==========================================
# STEP 1: Install AWS CLI
# ==========================================
pip install awscli
aws configure
# Enter: Access Key, Secret Key, Region

# ==========================================
# STEP 2: Create ECR repository
# ==========================================
aws ecr create-repository \
  --repository-name myapp \
  --region us-east-1

# ==========================================
# STEP 3: Login to ECR
# ==========================================
aws ecr get-login-password \
  --region us-east-1 | docker login \
  --username AWS \
  --password-stdin \
  123456789.dkr.ecr.us-east-1.amazonaws.com

# ==========================================
# STEP 4: Tag and Push
# ==========================================
docker tag myapp:latest \
  123456789.dkr.ecr.us-east-1.amazonaws.com/myapp:latest

docker push \
  123456789.dkr.ecr.us-east-1.amazonaws.com/myapp:latest
```

---

# 5) Complete Build + Tag + Push Script

```bash
#!/bin/bash
# push_docker.sh
# ==========================================
# Complete Docker build and push script
# ==========================================

# ==========================================
# Configuration
# ==========================================
APP_NAME="myapp"
VERSION="1.0.0"
DOCKER_HUB_USER="your-dockerhub-username"
ACR_NAME="myregistryname"
GITHUB_USER="your-github-username"

# ==========================================
# Colors for output
# ==========================================
GREEN='\033[0;32m'
BLUE='\033[0;34m'
RED='\033[0;31m'
NC='\033[0m'

echo -e "${BLUE}Starting Docker build and push...${NC}"

# ==========================================
# STEP 1: Build image
# ==========================================
echo -e "${BLUE}Building image...${NC}"
docker build -t ${APP_NAME}:${VERSION} .

if [ $? -ne 0 ]; then
    echo -e "${RED}Build failed!${NC}"
    exit 1
fi
echo -e "${GREEN}Build successful!${NC}"

# ==========================================
# STEP 2: Tag for all registries
# ==========================================
echo -e "${BLUE}Tagging images...${NC}"

# Docker Hub tags
docker tag ${APP_NAME}:${VERSION} \
  ${DOCKER_HUB_USER}/${APP_NAME}:${VERSION}
docker tag ${APP_NAME}:${VERSION} \
  ${DOCKER_HUB_USER}/${APP_NAME}:latest

# ACR tags
docker tag ${APP_NAME}:${VERSION} \
  ${ACR_NAME}.azurecr.io/${APP_NAME}:${VERSION}
docker tag ${APP_NAME}:${VERSION} \
  ${ACR_NAME}.azurecr.io/${APP_NAME}:latest

# GHCR tags
docker tag ${APP_NAME}:${VERSION} \
  ghcr.io/${GITHUB_USER}/${APP_NAME}:${VERSION}
docker tag ${APP_NAME}:${VERSION} \
  ghcr.io/${GITHUB_USER}/${APP_NAME}:latest

echo -e "${GREEN}Tagging done!${NC}"

# ==========================================
# STEP 3: Push to Docker Hub
# ==========================================
echo -e "${BLUE}Pushing to Docker Hub...${NC}"
docker push ${DOCKER_HUB_USER}/${APP_NAME}:${VERSION}
docker push ${DOCKER_HUB_USER}/${APP_NAME}:latest
echo -e "${GREEN}Docker Hub push done!${NC}"

# ==========================================
# STEP 4: Push to ACR
# ==========================================
echo -e "${BLUE}Pushing to ACR...${NC}"
az acr login --name ${ACR_NAME}
docker push ${ACR_NAME}.azurecr.io/${APP_NAME}:${VERSION}
docker push ${ACR_NAME}.azurecr.io/${APP_NAME}:latest
echo -e "${GREEN}ACR push done!${NC}"

# ==========================================
# STEP 5: Push to GHCR
# ==========================================
echo -e "${BLUE}Pushing to GHCR...${NC}"
docker push \
  ghcr.io/${GITHUB_USER}/${APP_NAME}:${VERSION}
docker push \
  ghcr.io/${GITHUB_USER}/${APP_NAME}:latest
echo -e "${GREEN}GHCR push done!${NC}"

echo -e "${GREEN}All pushes completed!${NC}"
```

```bash
# Make script executable and run
chmod +x push_docker.sh
./push_docker.sh
```

---

# 6) GitHub Actions - Auto Push on PR/Push

```yaml
# .github/workflows/docker-push.yml
# ==========================================
# Auto build and push Docker image
# Runs on every push to main
# ==========================================

name: Build and Push Docker Image

on:
  push:
    branches:
      - main
      - develop
  pull_request:
    branches:
      - main

env:
  APP_NAME: myapp
  ACR_NAME: myregistryname

jobs:

  # ==========================================
  # Job 1: Build and Test
  # ==========================================
  build-and-test:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest

    steps:
      - name: Checkout code
        uses: actions/checkout@v4

      - name: Set up Python
        uses: actions/setup-python@v5
        with:
          python-version: '3.12'

      - name: Install dependencies
        run: |
          pip install -r requirements.txt

      - name: Run tests
        run: |
          python -m pytest tests/ -v

  # ==========================================
  # Job 2: Build and Push Docker Image
  # ==========================================
  docker-build-push:
    runs-on: ubuntu-latest
    needs: build-and-test
    if: github.ref == 'refs/heads/main'

    steps:
      - name: Checkout code
        uses: actions/checkout@v4

      # ==========================================
      # Get version from git tag
      # ==========================================
      - name: Get version
        id: version
        run: |
          echo "VERSION=${GITHUB_SHA::8}" >> $GITHUB_OUTPUT
          echo "DATE=$(date +'%Y%m%d')" >> $GITHUB_OUTPUT

      # ==========================================
      # Push to Docker Hub
      # ==========================================
      - name: Login to Docker Hub
        uses: docker/login-action@v3
        with:
          username: ${{ secrets.DOCKERHUB_USERNAME }}
          password: ${{ secrets.DOCKERHUB_TOKEN }}

      - name: Build and push to Docker Hub
        uses: docker/build-push-action@v5
        with:
          context: .
          push: true
          tags: |
            ${{ secrets.DOCKERHUB_USERNAME }}/${{ env.APP_NAME }}:latest
            ${{ secrets.DOCKERHUB_USERNAME }}/${{ env.APP_NAME }}:${{ steps.version.outputs.VERSION }}

      # ==========================================
      # Push to Azure Container Registry
      # ==========================================
      - name: Login to Azure
        uses: azure/login@v1
        with:
          creds: ${{ secrets.AZURE_CREDENTIALS }}

      - name: Login to ACR
        run: |
          az acr login --name ${{ env.ACR_NAME }}

      - name: Build and push to ACR
        uses: docker/build-push-action@v5
        with:
          context: .
          push: true
          tags: |
            ${{ env.ACR_NAME }}.azurecr.io/${{ env.APP_NAME }}:latest
            ${{ env.ACR_NAME }}.azurecr.io/${{ env.APP_NAME }}:${{ steps.version.outputs.VERSION }}

      # ==========================================
      # Push to GitHub Container Registry
      # ==========================================
      - name: Login to GHCR
        uses: docker/login-action@v3
        with:
          registry: ghcr.io
          username: ${{ github.actor }}
          password: ${{ secrets.GITHUB_TOKEN }}

      - name: Build and push to GHCR
        uses: docker/build-push-action@v5
        with:
          context: .
          push: true
          tags: |
            ghcr.io/${{ github.repository_owner }}/${{ env.APP_NAME }}:latest
            ghcr.io/${{ github.repository_owner }}/${{ env.APP_NAME }}:${{ steps.version.outputs.VERSION }}

      # ==========================================
      # Summary
      # ==========================================
      - name: Print image details
        run: |
          echo "Images pushed:"
          echo "Docker Hub: ${{ secrets.DOCKERHUB_USERNAME }}/${{ env.APP_NAME }}:latest"
          echo "ACR: ${{ env.ACR_NAME }}.azurecr.io/${{ env.APP_NAME }}:latest"
          echo "GHCR: ghcr.io/${{ github.repository_owner }}/${{ env.APP_NAME }}:latest"
```

---

# 7) Setting up GitHub Secrets

```bash
# ==========================================
# Add secrets to GitHub repo:
# Go to: Repo → Settings → Secrets → Actions
# → New repository secret
# ==========================================

# Secrets to add:
DOCKERHUB_USERNAME    = your docker hub username
DOCKERHUB_TOKEN       = your docker hub token
AZURE_CREDENTIALS     = azure service principal JSON
ACR_USERNAME          = acr admin username
ACR_PASSWORD          = acr admin password

# Get Azure credentials
az ad sp create-for-rbac \
  --name "myapp-github-action" \
  --role contributor \
  --scopes /subscriptions/{subscription-id} \
  --sdk-auth

# Copy the JSON output and save as
# AZURE_CREDENTIALS secret
```

# 8) Pull and Run pushed image

```bash
# ==========================================
# Pull from Docker Hub
# ==========================================
docker pull your-username/myapp:latest
docker run -d -p 8000:8000 \
  your-username/myapp:latest

# ==========================================
# Pull from ACR
# ==========================================
az acr login --name myregistryname
docker pull \
  myregistryname.azurecr.io/myapp:latest
docker run -d -p 8000:8000 \
  myregistryname.azurecr.io/myapp:latest

# ==========================================
# Pull from GHCR
# ==========================================
docker pull \
  ghcr.io/your-github-username/myapp:latest
docker run -d -p 8000:8000 \
  ghcr.io/your-github-username/myapp:latest
```

---

# Quick revision sheet

```
BUILD IMAGE:
  docker build -t myapp .

TAG IMAGE:
  docker tag myapp username/myapp:latest

PUSH TO DOCKER HUB:
  docker login
  docker push username/myapp:latest

PUSH TO ACR:
  az acr login --name registryname
  docker tag myapp registryname.azurecr.io/myapp
  docker push registryname.azurecr.io/myapp

PUSH TO GHCR:
  docker login ghcr.io
  docker tag myapp ghcr.io/username/myapp
  docker push ghcr.io/username/myapp

PULL IMAGE:
  docker pull username/myapp:latest

TAG FORMAT:
  Docker Hub  → username/image:tag
  ACR         → name.azurecr.io/image:tag
  GHCR        → ghcr.io/username/image:tag
  ECR         → id.dkr.ecr.region.amazonaws.com/image:tag
```

# Interview-ready answers

## What is a container registry?
A storage system for Docker images where you push and pull images.

## What is image tagging?
Adding a label to an image like version number or latest to identify it.

## Why use ACR over Docker Hub?
ACR integrates with Azure services, private by default, and better for Azure deployments.

## What is latest tag?
A default tag pointing to the most recent version of an image.

## How to automate Docker push?
Using GitHub Actions workflow that builds and pushes on every code push.

# BASIC SQL QUESTIONS

---

# Q1) What is SQL?

## Answer
SQL = **Structured Query Language**

It is a language used to:
- create databases and tables
- insert data
- read data
- update data
- delete data
- manage databases

---

## Simple example
```sql
-- Create a table
CREATE TABLE students (
    id   INT,
    name VARCHAR(100),
    age  INT
);

-- Insert data
INSERT INTO students VALUES (1, 'Aman', 20);

-- Read data
SELECT * FROM students;

-- Update data
UPDATE students SET age = 21 WHERE id = 1;

-- Delete data
DELETE FROM students WHERE id = 1;
```

---

## Assignment 1
```
Create a table called "employees" with:
- id (integer)
- name (text, max 100 chars)
- salary (decimal)
- department (text)
- joining_date (date)

Then:
1. Insert 5 employees
2. Select all employees
3. Update salary of employee id 1
4. Delete employee id 5
```

---

# Q2) What are DDL, DML, DCL, TCL?

## Answer

```
DDL = Data Definition Language
      CREATE, ALTER, DROP, TRUNCATE
      Defines structure of database

DML = Data Manipulation Language
      SELECT, INSERT, UPDATE, DELETE
      Manipulates data

DCL = Data Control Language
      GRANT, REVOKE
      Controls permissions

TCL = Transaction Control Language
      COMMIT, ROLLBACK, SAVEPOINT
      Controls transactions
```

---

## Examples

```sql
-- ==========================================
-- DDL Examples
-- ==========================================

-- CREATE table
CREATE TABLE students (
    id   INT PRIMARY KEY,
    name VARCHAR(100)
);

-- ALTER table - add column
ALTER TABLE students
ADD email VARCHAR(100);

-- ALTER table - modify column
ALTER TABLE students
ALTER COLUMN name VARCHAR(200);

-- DROP table - deletes table permanently
DROP TABLE students;

-- TRUNCATE - deletes all data, keeps table
TRUNCATE TABLE students;

-- ==========================================
-- DML Examples
-- ==========================================

-- SELECT
SELECT * FROM students;

-- INSERT
INSERT INTO students (id, name)
VALUES (1, 'Aman');

-- UPDATE
UPDATE students
SET name = 'Aman Singh'
WHERE id = 1;

-- DELETE
DELETE FROM students
WHERE id = 1;

-- ==========================================
-- TCL Examples
-- ==========================================

-- Start transaction
BEGIN TRANSACTION;

-- Make changes
UPDATE students SET name = 'Test' WHERE id = 1;

-- Save changes
COMMIT;

-- Or undo changes
ROLLBACK;

-- Savepoint
SAVEPOINT before_update;
UPDATE students SET name = 'Test2' WHERE id = 2;
ROLLBACK TO before_update;
```

---

## Assignment 2
```
1. Create table "products" using DDL
2. Add a new column "category" using ALTER
3. Insert 5 products using DML
4. Update product price using DML
5. Start a transaction:
   - Update a product
   - Rollback the change
   - Verify data is unchanged
```

---

# Q3) What is SELECT with WHERE?

## Answer
SELECT reads data.
WHERE filters rows.

---

## Examples

```sql
-- ==========================================
-- Basic SELECT
-- ==========================================

-- All columns all rows
SELECT * FROM employees;

-- Specific columns
SELECT name, salary FROM employees;

-- With alias
SELECT
    name AS employee_name,
    salary AS monthly_salary
FROM employees;

-- ==========================================
-- WHERE conditions
-- ==========================================

-- Equal
SELECT * FROM employees
WHERE department = 'IT';

-- Greater than
SELECT * FROM employees
WHERE salary > 50000;

-- Less than
SELECT * FROM employees
WHERE age < 30;

-- Between
SELECT * FROM employees
WHERE salary BETWEEN 30000 AND 70000;

-- IN - multiple values
SELECT * FROM employees
WHERE department IN ('IT', 'HR', 'Finance');

-- NOT IN
SELECT * FROM employees
WHERE department NOT IN ('IT');

-- LIKE - pattern matching
-- % = any characters
-- _ = single character
SELECT * FROM employees
WHERE name LIKE 'A%';      -- starts with A

SELECT * FROM employees
WHERE name LIKE '%Singh';  -- ends with Singh

SELECT * FROM employees
WHERE name LIKE '%am%';    -- contains am

SELECT * FROM employees
WHERE name LIKE '_man';    -- 4 chars ending in man

-- NULL check
SELECT * FROM employees
WHERE email IS NULL;

SELECT * FROM employees
WHERE email IS NOT NULL;

-- Multiple conditions
SELECT * FROM employees
WHERE department = 'IT'
AND salary > 50000;

SELECT * FROM employees
WHERE department = 'IT'
OR department = 'HR';

-- Combine AND and OR
SELECT * FROM employees
WHERE (department = 'IT' OR department = 'HR')
AND salary > 40000;
```

---

## Assignment 3
```
Using employees table:
1. Select employees with salary > 50000
2. Select employees in IT or HR department
3. Select employees whose name starts with 'A'
4. Select employees joining between 2020-2023
5. Select employees where email is NULL
6. Select IT employees with salary between 40000-80000
7. Select employees NOT in Finance department
```

---

# Q4) What are ORDER BY and LIMIT?

## Answer
ORDER BY sorts results.
LIMIT restricts number of rows.

---

## Examples

```sql
-- ==========================================
-- ORDER BY
-- ==========================================

-- Sort ascending (default)
SELECT * FROM employees
ORDER BY salary;

-- Sort descending
SELECT * FROM employees
ORDER BY salary DESC;

-- Sort by multiple columns
SELECT * FROM employees
ORDER BY department ASC, salary DESC;

-- Sort by alias
SELECT name, salary * 12 AS annual_salary
FROM employees
ORDER BY annual_salary DESC;

-- ==========================================
-- LIMIT (MySQL/PostgreSQL)
-- ==========================================

-- First 5 rows
SELECT * FROM employees
LIMIT 5;

-- Skip 5, take next 5 (pagination)
SELECT * FROM employees
LIMIT 5 OFFSET 5;

-- Top 3 highest paid
SELECT * FROM employees
ORDER BY salary DESC
LIMIT 3;

-- ==========================================
-- TOP (SQL Server)
-- ==========================================

-- First 5 rows
SELECT TOP 5 * FROM employees;

-- Top 10 percent
SELECT TOP 10 PERCENT * FROM employees
ORDER BY salary DESC;

-- ==========================================
-- ROWNUM (Oracle)
-- ==========================================

SELECT * FROM employees
WHERE ROWNUM <= 5;
```

---

## Assignment 4
```
1. Get top 5 highest paid employees
2. Get bottom 3 lowest paid employees
3. Get employees sorted by department then name
4. Implement pagination:
   - Page 1: first 10 records
   - Page 2: next 10 records
   - Page 3: next 10 records
5. Get the 3rd highest salary employee
```

---

# Q5) What are Aggregate Functions?

## Answer
Aggregate functions perform calculations on multiple rows.

```
COUNT  → count rows
SUM    → add up values
AVG    → average value
MAX    → highest value
MIN    → lowest value
```

---

## Examples

```sql
-- ==========================================
-- Basic aggregates
-- ==========================================

-- Count all rows
SELECT COUNT(*) FROM employees;

-- Count non-null values
SELECT COUNT(email) FROM employees;

-- Count distinct values
SELECT COUNT(DISTINCT department)
FROM employees;

-- Sum of salaries
SELECT SUM(salary) FROM employees;

-- Average salary
SELECT AVG(salary) FROM employees;

-- Highest salary
SELECT MAX(salary) FROM employees;

-- Lowest salary
SELECT MIN(salary) FROM employees;

-- Multiple aggregates together
SELECT
    COUNT(*)        AS total_employees,
    SUM(salary)     AS total_salary,
    AVG(salary)     AS avg_salary,
    MAX(salary)     AS highest_salary,
    MIN(salary)     AS lowest_salary
FROM employees;

-- ==========================================
-- GROUP BY - aggregate per group
-- ==========================================

-- Count per department
SELECT
    department,
    COUNT(*) AS total
FROM employees
GROUP BY department;

-- Average salary per department
SELECT
    department,
    AVG(salary) AS avg_salary,
    MAX(salary) AS max_salary,
    MIN(salary) AS min_salary,
    COUNT(*)    AS total_employees
FROM employees
GROUP BY department;

-- ==========================================
-- HAVING - filter groups
-- Like WHERE but for groups
-- ==========================================

-- Departments with more than 5 employees
SELECT
    department,
    COUNT(*) AS total
FROM employees
GROUP BY department
HAVING COUNT(*) > 5;

-- Departments with avg salary > 50000
SELECT
    department,
    AVG(salary) AS avg_salary
FROM employees
GROUP BY department
HAVING AVG(salary) > 50000
ORDER BY avg_salary DESC;

-- ==========================================
-- WHERE vs HAVING
-- ==========================================

-- WHERE filters rows BEFORE grouping
-- HAVING filters groups AFTER grouping
SELECT
    department,
    AVG(salary) AS avg_salary
FROM employees
WHERE is_active = 1        -- filter rows first
GROUP BY department
HAVING AVG(salary) > 50000 -- then filter groups
ORDER BY avg_salary DESC;
```

---

## Assignment 5
```
1. Count total employees per department
2. Find average salary per department
3. Find department with highest total salary
4. Find departments with more than 3 employees
5. Find departments where avg salary > 60000
6. Find total salary cost per department
   only for active employees
7. Show department, employee count, avg salary,
   max salary - sorted by avg salary descending
```

---

# Q6) What are JOINs?

## Answer
JOINs combine rows from two or more tables.

```
INNER JOIN  → only matching rows
LEFT JOIN   → all left + matching right
RIGHT JOIN  → all right + matching left
FULL JOIN   → all rows from both
CROSS JOIN  → every combination
SELF JOIN   → join table with itself
```

---

## Setup tables

```sql
-- Employees table
CREATE TABLE employees (
    id            INT PRIMARY KEY,
    name          VARCHAR(100),
    department_id INT,
    salary        DECIMAL(10,2)
);

-- Departments table
CREATE TABLE departments (
    id       INT PRIMARY KEY,
    name     VARCHAR(100),
    location VARCHAR(100)
);

-- Insert data
INSERT INTO departments VALUES
(1, 'IT',      'Mumbai'),
(2, 'HR',      'Delhi'),
(3, 'Finance', 'Bangalore'),
(4, 'Marketing', 'Chennai');

INSERT INTO employees VALUES
(1, 'Aman',   1, 60000),
(2, 'Sara',   2, 50000),
(3, 'Raj',    1, 70000),
(4, 'Priya',  3, 55000),
(5, 'Ahmed',  NULL, 45000);  -- no department
```

---

## JOIN Examples

```sql
-- ==========================================
-- INNER JOIN
-- Only rows that match in BOTH tables
-- ==========================================
SELECT
    e.name,
    e.salary,
    d.name AS department,
    d.location
FROM employees e
INNER JOIN departments d
    ON e.department_id = d.id;

-- Result: Ahmed excluded (no department)
-- Marketing excluded (no employees)

-- ==========================================
-- LEFT JOIN
-- All employees + matching department
-- ==========================================
SELECT
    e.name,
    e.salary,
    d.name AS department
FROM employees e
LEFT JOIN departments d
    ON e.department_id = d.id;

-- Result: Ahmed included with NULL department

-- ==========================================
-- RIGHT JOIN
-- All departments + matching employees
-- ==========================================
SELECT
    e.name,
    d.name AS department
FROM employees e
RIGHT JOIN departments d
    ON e.department_id = d.id;

-- Result: Marketing included with NULL employee

-- ==========================================
-- FULL OUTER JOIN
-- All rows from both tables
-- ==========================================
SELECT
    e.name,
    d.name AS department
FROM employees e
FULL OUTER JOIN departments d
    ON e.department_id = d.id;

-- Result: Both Ahmed and Marketing included

-- ==========================================
-- CROSS JOIN
-- Every employee with every department
-- ==========================================
SELECT
    e.name,
    d.name AS department
FROM employees e
CROSS JOIN departments d;

-- Result: 5 employees × 4 departments = 20 rows

-- ==========================================
-- SELF JOIN
-- Employee and their manager
-- ==========================================
SELECT
    e.name AS employee,
    m.name AS manager
FROM employees e
LEFT JOIN employees m
    ON e.manager_id = m.id;

-- ==========================================
-- Multiple JOINs
-- ==========================================
SELECT
    e.name,
    d.name    AS department,
    p.title   AS project
FROM employees e
JOIN departments d ON e.department_id = d.id
JOIN projects p    ON e.project_id = p.id;
```

```
Create these tables:
- students (id, name, course_id, marks)
- courses (id, title, teacher_id)
- teachers (id, name, subject)

Then:
1. INNER JOIN: students with their courses
2. LEFT JOIN: all students even without course
3. RIGHT JOIN: all courses even without students

5. Find students with marks > 80 and their
   course names and teacher names
6. Count students per course using JOIN
7. Find courses with no students enrolled
```

# Q7) What are Subqueries?

## Answer
A subquery is a **query inside another query**.

---

## Examples

```sql
-- ==========================================
-- Subquery in WHERE
-- ==========================================

-- Employees earning more than average
SELECT name, salary
FROM employees
WHERE salary > (
    SELECT AVG(salary)
    FROM employees
);

-- Employees in IT department
SELECT name
FROM employees
WHERE department_id = (
    SELECT id
    FROM departments
    WHERE name = 'IT'
);

-- ==========================================
-- Subquery with IN
-- ==========================================

-- Employees in specific departments
SELECT name
FROM employees
WHERE department_id IN (
    SELECT id
    FROM departments
    WHERE location = 'Mumbai'
);

-- ==========================================
-- Subquery in SELECT
-- ==========================================

-- Show employee salary vs avg salary
SELECT
    name,
    salary,
    (SELECT AVG(salary) FROM employees) AS avg_salary,
    salary - (SELECT AVG(salary) FROM employees)
        AS diff_from_avg
FROM employees;

-- ==========================================
-- Correlated Subquery
-- Runs for each row of outer query
-- ==========================================

-- Employees earning more than
-- avg salary of their department
SELECT name, salary, department_id
FROM employees e1
WHERE salary > (
    SELECT AVG(salary)
    FROM employees e2
    WHERE e2.department_id = e1.department_id
);

-- ==========================================
-- EXISTS
-- ==========================================

-- Departments that have employees
SELECT name
FROM departments d
WHERE EXISTS (
    SELECT 1
    FROM employees e
    WHERE e.department_id = d.id
);

-- Departments with NO employees
SELECT name
FROM departments d
WHERE NOT EXISTS (
    SELECT 1
    FROM employees e
    WHERE e.department_id = d.id
);
```

---

## Assignment 7
```
1. Find employees with salary above company average
2. Find the highest paid employee in each department
3. Find departments that have at least one employee
4. Find employees who earn more than all employees
   in HR department
5. Find the 2nd highest salary using subquery
6. Find students who scored above their class average
7. List products more expensive than average
   price in their category
```

---

# Q8) What are Window Functions?

## Answer
Window functions perform calculations **across related rows** without grouping.

---

## Examples

```sql
-- ==========================================
-- ROW_NUMBER
-- Assigns unique number to each row
-- ==========================================
SELECT
    name,
    salary,
    department_id,
    ROW_NUMBER() OVER (
        PARTITION BY department_id
        ORDER BY salary DESC
    ) AS rank_in_dept
FROM employees;

-- ==========================================
-- RANK
-- Same value gets same rank
-- Skips numbers after tie
-- ==========================================
SELECT
    name,
    salary,
    RANK() OVER (
        ORDER BY salary DESC
    ) AS salary_rank
FROM employees;

-- ==========================================
-- DENSE_RANK
-- Same value gets same rank
-- Does NOT skip numbers
-- ==========================================
SELECT
    name,
    salary,
    DENSE_RANK() OVER (
        ORDER BY salary DESC
    ) AS salary_rank
FROM employees;

-- ==========================================
-- LEAD and LAG
-- Access next/previous row value
-- ==========================================

-- Next employee's salary
SELECT
    name,
    salary,
    LEAD(salary) OVER (
        ORDER BY salary
    ) AS next_salary

FROM employees;

-- Previous employee's salary
SELECT
    name,
    salary,
    LAG(salary) OVER (
        ORDER BY salary
    ) AS prev_salary
FROM employees;

-- ==========================================
-- SUM OVER - Running total
-- ==========================================
SELECT
    name,
    salary,
    SUM(salary) OVER (
        ORDER BY id
        ROWS BETWEEN UNBOUNDED PRECEDING
        AND CURRENT ROW
    ) AS running_total
FROM employees;

-- ==========================================
-- FIRST_VALUE and LAST_VALUE
-- ==========================================
SELECT
    name,
    salary,
    department_id,
    FIRST_VALUE(name) OVER (
        PARTITION BY department_id
        ORDER BY salary DESC
    ) AS highest_paid_in_dept
FROM employees;

-- ==========================================
-- NTILE - divide into buckets
-- ==========================================
SELECT
    name,
    salary,
    NTILE(4) OVER (
        ORDER BY salary
    ) AS salary_quartile
FROM employees;
```

---

## Assignment 8
```
1. Rank employees by salary within each department
2. Find top 2 earners per department using ROW_NUMBER
3. Show running total of salaries ordered by date
4. Compare each employee salary with
   previous and next employee
5. Find employees in top 25% salary using NTILE
6. Show each sale and the previous month's sale
7. Find the highest paid employee name
   for each department using FIRST_VALUE
```

# Q9) What are Indexes?

## Answer
An index makes **data retrieval faster**.

Like index in a book - no need to read everything.

---

## Examples

```sql
-- ==========================================
-- CREATE INDEX
-- ==========================================

-- Basic index
CREATE INDEX idx_employee_name
ON employees(name);

-- Unique index
CREATE UNIQUE INDEX idx_employee_email
ON employees(email);

-- Composite index (multiple columns)
CREATE INDEX idx_dept_salary
ON employees(department_id, salary);

-- ==========================================
-- VIEW INDEXES
-- ==========================================

-- MySQL
SHOW INDEX FROM employees;

-- SQL Server
SELECT * FROM sys.indexes
WHERE object_id = OBJECT_ID('employees');

-- ==========================================
-- DROP INDEX
-- ==========================================

-- MySQL
DROP INDEX idx_employee_name
ON employees;

-- SQL Server
DROP INDEX employees.idx_employee_name;

-- ==========================================
-- When to use indexes
-- ==========================================

-- Good for:
-- columns used in WHERE
SELECT * FROM employees WHERE email = 'a@b.com';

-- columns used in JOIN
SELECT * FROM employees e
JOIN departments d ON e.department_id = d.id;

-- columns used in ORDER BY
SELECT * FROM employees ORDER BY salary;

-- ==========================================
-- When NOT to use indexes
-- ==========================================

-- Small tables
-- Columns with low cardinality (few unique values)
-- Columns updated very frequently
-- Too many indexes slow down INSERT/UPDATE/DELETE
```

---

## Assignment 9
```
1. Create index on employees email column
2. Create composite index on
   (department_id, salary)
3. Create unique index on student roll number
4. Write queries that USE these indexes
   (use WHERE on indexed columns)
5. Check if query uses index:
   EXPLAIN SELECT * FROM employees
   WHERE email = 'test@email.com'
6. Explain when you would NOT create an index
```

---

# Q10) What are Stored Procedures?

## Answer
A stored procedure is a **saved SQL code** you can reuse.

Like a function in programming.

---

## Examples

```sql
-- ==========================================
-- CREATE PROCEDURE (SQL Server)
-- ==========================================

-- Simple procedure
CREATE PROCEDURE GetAllEmployees
AS
BEGIN
    SELECT * FROM employees;
END;

-- Execute procedure
EXEC GetAllEmployees;

-- ==========================================
-- Procedure with parameters
-- ==========================================
CREATE PROCEDURE GetEmployeesByDept
    @department_name VARCHAR(100)
AS
BEGIN
    SELECT e.name, e.salary
    FROM employees e
    JOIN departments d
        ON e.department_id = d.id
    WHERE d.name = @department_name;
END;

-- Execute with parameter
EXEC GetEmployeesByDept @department_name = 'IT';

-- ==========================================
-- Procedure with OUTPUT parameter
-- ==========================================
CREATE PROCEDURE GetEmployeeCount
    @department_name VARCHAR(100),
    @count INT OUTPUT
AS
BEGIN
    SELECT @count = COUNT(*)
    FROM employees e
    JOIN departments d
        ON e.department_id = d.id
    WHERE d.name = @department_name;
END;

-- Execute with output
DECLARE @total INT;
EXEC GetEmployeeCount
    @department_name = 'IT',
    @count = @total OUTPUT;
SELECT @total AS IT_Count;

-- ==========================================
-- MySQL Procedure
-- ==========================================
DELIMITER //
CREATE PROCEDURE GetHighEarners(
    IN min_salary DECIMAL(10,2)
)
BEGIN
    SELECT name, salary
    FROM employees
    WHERE salary >= min_salary
    ORDER BY salary DESC;
END //
DELIMITER ;

-- Call MySQL procedure
CALL GetHighEarners(60000);
```

---

## Assignment 10
```
1. Create procedure to get all employees
2. Create procedure to get employees by department
3. Create procedure to insert new employee
   with validation (salary must be > 0)
4. Create procedure to give salary raise
   to all employees in a department
5. Create procedure that returns:
   - total employees
   - avg salary
   - max salary
   for a given department
```

# ADVANCED QUESTIONS

---

# Q11) What are CTEs?

## Answer
CTE = **Common Table Expression**

A temporary named result set you can reference in a query.

---

## Examples

```sql
-- ==========================================
-- Basic CTE
-- ==========================================
WITH high_earners AS (
    SELECT name, salary, department_id
    FROM employees
    WHERE salary > 60000
)
SELECT *
FROM high_earners
ORDER BY salary DESC;

-- ==========================================
-- Multiple CTEs
-- ==========================================
WITH
dept_avg AS (
    SELECT
        department_id,
        AVG(salary) AS avg_sal
    FROM employees
    GROUP BY department_id
),
above_avg AS (
    SELECT e.name, e.salary, e.department_id
    FROM employees e
    JOIN dept_avg d
        ON e.department_id = d.department_id
    WHERE e.salary > d.avg_sal
)
SELECT * FROM above_avg;

-- ==========================================
-- Recursive CTE
-- Great for hierarchies
-- ==========================================

-- Employee hierarchy
WITH RECURSIVE emp_hierarchy AS (
    -- Base case: top level (no manager)
    SELECT
        id,
        name,
        manager_id,
        0 AS level
    FROM employees
    WHERE manager_id IS NULL

    UNION ALL

    -- Recursive case: add subordinates
    SELECT
        e.id,
        e.name,
        e.manager_id,
        h.level + 1
    FROM employees e
    JOIN emp_hierarchy h
        ON e.manager_id = h.id
)
SELECT * FROM emp_hierarchy
ORDER BY level, name;
```

---

## Assignment 11
```
1. Use CTE to find top 3 earners per department
2. Use CTE to calculate running total of sales
3. Create recursive CTE to show
   employee reporting hierarchy
4. Use multiple CTEs to find departments
   where all employees earn above average
5. Replace a complex subquery with a CTE
   and explain the difference
```

---

# Q12) What is Query Optimization?

## Answer
Making queries run **faster and use less resources**.

---

## Examples

```sql
-- ==========================================
-- BAD vs GOOD queries
-- ==========================================

-- BAD: SELECT *
SELECT * FROM employees;

-- GOOD: Select only needed columns
SELECT id, name, salary FROM employees;

-- ==========================================
-- BAD: Function on indexed column
-- ==========================================
SELECT * FROM employees
WHERE YEAR(joining_date) = 2023;
-- Index on joining_date NOT used!

-- GOOD: Use range instead
SELECT * FROM employees
WHERE joining_date >= '2023-01-01'
AND joining_date < '2024-01-01';
-- Index IS used!

-- ==========================================
-- BAD: OR on different columns
-- ==========================================
SELECT * FROM employees
WHERE name = 'Aman'
OR email = 'aman@email.com';

-- GOOD: Use UNION
SELECT * FROM employees WHERE name = 'Aman'
UNION
SELECT * FROM employees WHERE email = 'aman@email.com';

-- ==========================================
-- BAD: Correlated subquery
-- Runs for every row!
-- ==========================================
SELECT name, salary
FROM employees e
WHERE salary > (
    SELECT AVG(salary)
    FROM employees
    WHERE department_id = e.department_id
);

-- GOOD: Use CTE or JOIN
WITH dept_avg AS (
    SELECT department_id, AVG(salary) AS avg_sal
    FROM employees
    GROUP BY department_id
)
SELECT e.name, e.salary
FROM employees e
JOIN dept_avg d
    ON e.department_id = d.department_id
WHERE e.salary > d.avg_sal;

-- ==========================================
-- EXPLAIN - see query execution plan
-- ==========================================
EXPLAIN SELECT * FROM employees
WHERE department_id = 1;
```

---

## Assignment 12
```
1. Take a slow query and optimize it:
   SELECT * FROM orders
   WHERE YEAR(order_date) = 2023
   AND MONTH(order_date) = 6;

2. Rewrite this without correlated subquery:
   SELECT * FROM products p
   WHERE price > (
       SELECT AVG(price) FROM products
       WHERE category = p.category
   );

3. Add proper indexes to optimize:
   SELECT * FROM employees
   WHERE department = 'IT'
   AND salary > 50000
   ORDER BY name;

4. Use EXPLAIN to compare
   two versions of same query

5. Optimize this:
   SELECT * FROM customers
   WHERE UPPER(email) = 'TEST@EMAIL.COM';
```

---

# Quick revision sheet

```
BASIC:
  SELECT     → read data
  WHERE      → filter rows
  ORDER BY   → sort results
  LIMIT      → restrict rows
  GROUP BY   → group rows
  HAVING     → filter groups
  JOIN       → combine tables
  SUBQUERY   → query in query

INTERMEDIATE:
  WINDOW FUNCTIONS → calculations across rows
  INDEX            → faster data retrieval
  STORED PROCEDURE → saved reusable SQL

ADVANCED:
  CTE              → named temporary result
  RECURSIVE CTE    → hierarchical data
  QUERY OPTIMIZATION → faster queries
```

```sql
-- ==========================================
-- CREATE DATABASE
-- ==========================================
CREATE DATABASE school_management;
USE school_management;

-- ==========================================
-- TABLE 1: DEPARTMENTS
-- ==========================================
CREATE TABLE departments (
    id          INT PRIMARY KEY AUTO_INCREMENT,
    name        VARCHAR(100) NOT NULL,
    location    VARCHAR(100),
    budget      DECIMAL(12,2),
    established_year INT,
    head_name   VARCHAR(100)
);

-- ==========================================
-- TABLE 4: COURSES
-- ==========================================
CREATE TABLE courses (
    id            INT PRIMARY KEY AUTO_INCREMENT,
    code          VARCHAR(20) UNIQUE,
    title         VARCHAR(200) NOT NULL,
    department_id INT,
    teacher_id    INT,
    credits       INT DEFAULT 3,
    max_students  INT DEFAULT 30,
    fee           DECIMAL(10,2),
    semester      VARCHAR(20),
    is_active     BOOLEAN DEFAULT TRUE,
    FOREIGN KEY (department_id)
        REFERENCES departments(id),
    FOREIGN KEY (teacher_id)
        REFERENCES teachers(id)
);

-- ==========================================
-- TABLE 5: ENROLLMENTS
-- ==========================================
CREATE TABLE enrollments (
    id            INT PRIMARY KEY AUTO_INCREMENT,
    student_id    INT,
    course_id     INT,
    enrolled_date DATE,
    status        VARCHAR(20) DEFAULT 'active',
    FOREIGN KEY (student_id) REFERENCES students(id),
    FOREIGN KEY (course_id)  REFERENCES courses(id)
);

-- TABLE 6: EXAMS
-- ==========================================
CREATE TABLE exams (
    id          INT PRIMARY KEY AUTO_INCREMENT,
    course_id   INT,
    exam_name   VARCHAR(100),
    exam_date   DATE,
    total_marks INT,
    exam_type   VARCHAR(50),
    FOREIGN KEY (course_id) REFERENCES courses(id)
);

-- ==========================================
-- TABLE 7: RESULTS
-- ==========================================
CREATE TABLE results (
    id           INT PRIMARY KEY AUTO_INCREMENT,
    student_id   INT,
    exam_id      INT,
    marks        DECIMAL(5,2),
    grade        VARCHAR(5),
    remarks      VARCHAR(100),
    FOREIGN KEY (student_id) REFERENCES students(id),
    FOREIGN KEY (exam_id)    REFERENCES exams(id)
);

-- ==========================================
-- TABLE 8: ATTENDANCE
-- ==========================================
CREATE TABLE attendance (
    id           INT PRIMARY KEY AUTO_INCREMENT,
    student_id   INT,
    course_id    INT,
    date         DATE,
    status       VARCHAR(10),
    FOREIGN KEY (student_id) REFERENCES students(id),
    FOREIGN KEY (course_id)  REFERENCES courses(id)
);

INSERT INTO attendance VALUES

    id             INT PRIMARY KEY AUTO_INCREMENT,

    FOREIGN KEY (student_id) REFERENCES students(id)
);
```

# Now let's cover each topic

---

# TOPIC 1: SELECT and WHERE

## Explanation
```
SELECT = choose which columns to show
FROM   = which table to get data from
WHERE  = filter which rows to show
```

---

## Examples on our database

```sql
-- ==========================================
-- Basic SELECT
-- ==========================================

-- All students
SELECT * FROM students;

-- Only names and emails
SELECT name, email FROM students;

-- With column alias
SELECT
    name        AS student_name,
    roll_number AS roll_no,
    city        AS home_city
FROM students;

-- ==========================================
-- WHERE conditions
-- ==========================================

-- Students from Mumbai
SELECT name, city
FROM students
WHERE city = 'Mumbai';

-- Active students only
SELECT name, roll_number
FROM students
WHERE is_active = TRUE;

-- Students admitted in 2021
SELECT name, admission_year
FROM students
WHERE admission_year = 2021;

-- Students aged between conditions
SELECT name, date_of_birth
FROM students
WHERE date_of_birth > '2002-01-01';

-- Male students from Delhi
SELECT name, gender, city
FROM students
WHERE gender = 'Male'
AND city = 'Delhi';

-- Students from Mumbai OR Delhi
SELECT name, city
FROM students
WHERE city = 'Mumbai'
OR city = 'Delhi';

-- Students using IN
SELECT name, city
FROM students
WHERE city IN ('Mumbai', 'Delhi', 'Pune');

-- Students NOT in Mumbai
SELECT name, city
FROM students
WHERE city NOT IN ('Mumbai');

-- Students whose name starts with A
SELECT name FROM students
WHERE name LIKE 'A%';

-- Students whose name contains 'ar'
SELECT name FROM students
WHERE name LIKE '%ar%';

-- Students with NULL email
SELECT name, email
FROM students
WHERE email IS NULL;

-- Fees that are pending or overdue
SELECT student_id, status, amount
FROM fees
WHERE status IN ('pending', 'overdue');

-- Results with marks between 70 and 90
SELECT student_id, marks, grade
FROM results
WHERE marks BETWEEN 70 AND 90;
```

---

## Interview Questions

```
Q1: What is difference between WHERE and HAVING?
Q2: Can we use WHERE with aggregate functions?
Q3: What is difference between = and LIKE?
Q4: What does IS NULL check?
Q5: What is difference between AND and OR?
```

---

## Assignments

```sql
-- YOUR ANSWER:

```

# TOPIC 2: ORDER BY and LIMIT

## Explanation
```
ORDER BY = sort rows
  ASC  = smallest to largest (default)
  DESC = largest to smallest

LIMIT = how many rows to return
OFFSET = how many rows to skip
```

---

## Examples

```sql
-- Students sorted by name A to Z
SELECT name, city
FROM students
ORDER BY name ASC;

-- Teachers sorted by salary highest first
SELECT name, salary
FROM teachers
ORDER BY salary DESC;

-- Sort by multiple columns
SELECT name, department_id, salary
FROM teachers
ORDER BY department_id ASC, salary DESC;

-- Top 5 highest paid teachers
SELECT name, salary
FROM teachers
ORDER BY salary DESC
LIMIT 5;

-- Bottom 3 lowest paid teachers
SELECT name, salary
FROM teachers
ORDER BY salary ASC
LIMIT 3;

-- Page 1: students 1-10
SELECT name, roll_number
FROM students
ORDER BY id
LIMIT 10 OFFSET 0;

-- Page 2: students 11-20
SELECT name, roll_number
FROM students
ORDER BY id
LIMIT 10 OFFSET 10;

-- Top 3 results with highest marks
SELECT student_id, marks, grade
FROM results
ORDER BY marks DESC
LIMIT 3;

-- Courses sorted by fee highest to lowest
SELECT title, fee
FROM courses
ORDER BY fee DESC;

-- Most recent fee payments
SELECT student_id, amount, paid_date
FROM fees
WHERE status = 'paid'
ORDER BY paid_date DESC
LIMIT 5;
```

---

# TOPIC 3: GROUP BY and Aggregate Functions

## Explanation
```
COUNT → count rows
SUM   → total
AVG   → average
MAX   → highest
MIN   → lowest

GROUP BY  → group rows for aggregation
HAVING    → filter groups
```

---

## Examples

```sql
-- Total number of students
SELECT COUNT(*) AS total_students
FROM students;

-- Students per department
SELECT
    d.name AS department,
    COUNT(s.id) AS total_students
FROM students s
JOIN departments d
    ON s.department_id = d.id
GROUP BY d.name
ORDER BY total_students DESC;

-- Average salary per department
SELECT
    d.name,
    ROUND(AVG(t.salary), 2) AS avg_salary,
    MAX(t.salary) AS max_salary,
    MIN(t.salary) AS min_salary,
    COUNT(t.id)   AS total_teachers
FROM teachers t
JOIN departments d
    ON t.department_id = d.id
GROUP BY d.name;

-- Total fees collected per year
SELECT
    YEAR(paid_date) AS year,
    COUNT(*)        AS payments,
    SUM(amount)     AS total_collected
FROM fees
WHERE status = 'paid'
GROUP BY YEAR(paid_date)
ORDER BY year;

-- Departments with more than 2 teachers
SELECT
    d.name,
    COUNT(t.id) AS teacher_count
FROM teachers t
JOIN departments d
    ON t.department_id = d.id
GROUP BY d.name
HAVING COUNT(t.id) > 2;

-- Average marks per course
SELECT
    c.title,
    ROUND(AVG(r.marks), 2) AS avg_marks,
    COUNT(r.id)            AS total_results
FROM results r
JOIN exams e  ON r.exam_id = e.id
JOIN courses c ON e.course_id = c.id
WHERE e.exam_type = 'final'
GROUP BY c.title
ORDER BY avg_marks DESC;

-- Count students by gender
SELECT
    gender,
    COUNT(*) AS count
FROM students
GROUP BY gender;

-- Total fine per student
SELECT
    s.name,
    SUM(l.fine) AS total_fine
FROM library l
JOIN students s ON l.student_id = s.id
WHERE l.fine > 0
GROUP BY s.name
ORDER BY total_fine DESC;
```

---

```sql
-- YOUR ANSWER:

-- YOUR ANSWER:

-- YOUR ANSWER:

-- YOUR ANSWER:

```

---

# TOPIC 4: JOINS

## Explanation
```
INNER JOIN  → matching rows only
LEFT JOIN   → all left + matched right
RIGHT JOIN  → all right + matched left
FULL JOIN   → all rows both sides
SELF JOIN   → table joins itself
```

---

## Examples

```sql
-- INNER JOIN: Students with department names
SELECT
    s.name,
    s.roll_number,
    d.name AS department
FROM students s
INNER JOIN departments d
    ON s.department_id = d.id;

-- LEFT JOIN: All students even without dept
SELECT
    s.name,
    d.name AS department
FROM students s
LEFT JOIN departments d
    ON s.department_id = d.id;

-- Students with course names
SELECT
    s.name      AS student,
    c.title     AS course,
    e.status
FROM enrollments e
JOIN students s ON e.student_id = s.id
JOIN courses c  ON e.course_id = c.id
ORDER BY s.name;

-- Teachers with their departments
SELECT
    t.name       AS teacher,
    t.salary,
    d.name       AS department,
    d.location
FROM teachers t
JOIN departments d
    ON t.department_id = d.id;

-- Students results with course names
SELECT
    s.name        AS student,
    c.title       AS course,
    ex.exam_name,
    r.marks,
    r.grade
FROM results r
JOIN students s ON r.student_id = s.id
JOIN exams ex   ON r.exam_id = ex.id
JOIN courses c  ON ex.course_id = c.id
ORDER BY s.name, r.marks DESC;

-- Courses with NO enrollments
SELECT c.title
FROM courses c
LEFT JOIN enrollments e
    ON c.id = e.course_id
WHERE e.id IS NULL;

-- Students with their library books
SELECT
    s.name,
    l.book_name,
    l.status,
    l.fine
FROM students s
LEFT JOIN library l
    ON s.student_id = l.student_id;

-- RIGHT JOIN: All courses and enrolled students
SELECT
    c.title,
    s.name AS student
FROM students s
RIGHT JOIN enrollments e ON s.id = e.student_id
RIGHT JOIN courses c     ON e.course_id = c.id;
```

---

# TOPIC 5: Subqueries

## Explanation
```
Subquery = query inside query

Types:
1. Subquery in WHERE
2. Subquery in SELECT
3. Subquery in FROM
4. Correlated subquery
```

---

## Examples

```sql
-- Students in Computer Science
SELECT name
FROM students
WHERE department_id = (
    SELECT id
    FROM departments
    WHERE name = 'Computer Science'
);

-- Teachers earning above average salary
SELECT name, salary
FROM teachers
WHERE salary > (
    SELECT AVG(salary) FROM teachers
);

-- Students with highest marks
SELECT s.name, r.marks
FROM results r
JOIN students s ON r.student_id = s.id
WHERE r.marks = (
    SELECT MAX(marks) FROM results
);

-- Courses with more than avg enrollments
SELECT title
FROM courses
WHERE id IN (
    SELECT course_id
    FROM enrollments
    GROUP BY course_id
    HAVING COUNT(*) > (
        SELECT AVG(cnt)
        FROM (
            SELECT COUNT(*) AS cnt
            FROM enrollments
            GROUP BY course_id
        ) AS sub
    )
);

-- Correlated: Students earning above
-- their department average
SELECT
    s.name,
    r.marks,
    s.department_id
FROM results r
JOIN students s ON r.student_id = s.id
WHERE r.marks > (
    SELECT AVG(r2.marks)
    FROM results r2
    JOIN students s2
        ON r2.student_id = s2.id
    WHERE s2.department_id = s.department_id
);

-- Students with pending fees
SELECT name
FROM students
WHERE id IN (
    SELECT student_id
    FROM fees
    WHERE status = 'pending'
);

-- Departments with no students
SELECT name
FROM departments
WHERE id NOT IN (
    SELECT DISTINCT department_id
    FROM students
    WHERE department_id IS NOT NULL
);
```

---

## Assignments

```sql

-- YOUR ANSWER:

-- YOUR ANSWER:
```

# TOPIC 6: Window Functions

## Explanation
```
ROW_NUMBER  → unique number per row
RANK        → rank with gaps
DENSE_RANK  → rank without gaps
LAG         → previous row value
LEAD        → next row value
SUM OVER    → running total
FIRST_VALUE → first value in window
NTILE       → divide into buckets
```

---

## Examples

```sql
-- Rank teachers by salary
SELECT
    name,
    salary,
    department_id,
    RANK() OVER (
        ORDER BY salary DESC
    ) AS overall_rank
FROM teachers;

-- Rank within each department
SELECT
    t.name,
    t.salary,
    d.name AS department,
    DENSE_RANK() OVER (
        PARTITION BY t.department_id
        ORDER BY t.salary DESC
    ) AS dept_rank
FROM teachers t
JOIN departments d
    ON t.department_id = d.id;

-- Top 1 teacher per department
SELECT *
FROM (
    SELECT
        t.name,
        t.salary,
        d.name AS department,
        ROW_NUMBER() OVER (
            PARTITION BY t.department_id
            ORDER BY t.salary DESC
        ) AS rn
    FROM teachers t
    JOIN departments d
        ON t.department_id = d.id
) ranked
WHERE rn = 1;

-- Running total of fees collected
SELECT
    student_id,
    paid_date,
    amount,
    SUM(amount) OVER (
        ORDER BY paid_date
        ROWS BETWEEN UNBOUNDED PRECEDING
        AND CURRENT ROW
    ) AS running_total
FROM fees
WHERE status = 'paid';

-- Compare marks with previous exam
SELECT
    s.name,
    ex.exam_name,
    r.marks,
    LAG(r.marks) OVER (
        PARTITION BY r.student_id
        ORDER BY ex.exam_date
    ) AS prev_marks,
    r.marks - LAG(r.marks) OVER (
        PARTITION BY r.student_id
        ORDER BY ex.exam_date
    ) AS improvement
FROM results r
JOIN students s ON r.student_id = s.id
JOIN exams ex   ON r.exam_id = ex.id;

-- Divide teachers into salary quartiles
SELECT
    name,
    salary,
    NTILE(4) OVER (
        ORDER BY salary
    ) AS quartile
FROM teachers;
```

---

# TOPIC 7: CTEs

## Explanation
```
CTE = Common Table Expression
WITH name AS (query)

Like creating a temp table
Makes complex queries readable
Can be reused in same query
Can be recursive
```

---

## Examples

```sql
-- Simple CTE
WITH high_salary_teachers AS (
    SELECT name, salary, department_id
    FROM teachers
    WHERE salary > 65000
)
SELECT * FROM high_salary_teachers
ORDER BY salary DESC;

-- Multiple CTEs
WITH
dept_stats AS (
    SELECT
        department_id,
        COUNT(*) AS teacher_count,
        AVG(salary) AS avg_salary
    FROM teachers
    GROUP BY department_id
),
high_performing_depts AS (
    SELECT department_id
    FROM dept_stats
    WHERE avg_salary > 65000
    AND teacher_count >= 2
)
SELECT
    d.name,
    ds.teacher_count,
    ds.avg_salary
FROM departments d
JOIN dept_stats ds
    ON d.id = ds.department_id
JOIN high_performing_depts hpd
    ON d.id = hpd.department_id;

-- CTE for top students
WITH student_totals AS (
    SELECT
        s.name,
        s.department_id,
        SUM(r.marks)  AS total_marks,
        AVG(r.marks)  AS avg_marks,
        COUNT(r.id)   AS exams_taken
    FROM students s
    JOIN results r ON s.id = r.student_id
    GROUP BY s.id, s.name, s.department_id
),
ranked_students AS (
    SELECT *,
        RANK() OVER (
            ORDER BY total_marks DESC
        ) AS overall_rank
    FROM student_totals
)
SELECT *
FROM ranked_students
WHERE overall_rank <= 5;

-- Recursive CTE for department hierarchy
WITH RECURSIVE dept_tree AS (
    SELECT
        id,
        name,
        1 AS level
    FROM departments
    WHERE id = 1

    UNION ALL

    SELECT
        d.id,
        d.name,
        dt.level + 1
    FROM departments d
    JOIN dept_tree dt
        ON d.id = dt.id + 1
    WHERE dt.level < 3
)
SELECT * FROM dept_tree;
```

---

## Assignments

```sql
-- Assignment 7:

-- 1. Use CTE to find students with
-- above average marks
-- YOUR ANSWER:


-- 2. Use CTE to find top teacher
```

```sql
-- 2. Students admitted before 2021

-- 3. Teachers with salary above 65000

-- 4. Courses with more than 35 max students

-- 5. Students from Hyderabad or Chennai
SELECT * FROM students
WHERE city IN ('Hyderabad', 'Chennai');

-- 6. Fees that are NOT paid
SELECT * FROM fees WHERE status != 'paid';

-- 7. Library books that are overdue
SELECT * FROM library WHERE status = 'overdue';

WHERE joining_date > '2018-12-31';

-- 10. Results where grade is A or A+
```

# Quick revision sheet

```
SELECT  → choose columns
WHERE   → filter rows
ORDER BY→ sort
LIMIT   → restrict rows
GROUP BY→ group for aggregation
HAVING  → filter groups
JOIN    → combine tables
Subquery→ query inside query
CTE     → named temp result
Window  → calculations across rows
```

# Question

```sql
-- Find all students admitted before 2021
```

# Answer

# Line by line explanation

```sql
SELECT *
```
```
SELECT = read data from table
*      = give me ALL columns
         id, name, email, phone,
         date_of_birth, gender,
         address, city, department_id,
         admission_year, roll_number,
         is_active, guardian_name,
         guardian_phone

If you only want specific columns:
SELECT id, name, admission_year
```

---

```sql
FROM students
```
```
FROM     = which table to read from
students = our students table

This is where all student data lives
```

---

```sql
WHERE admission_year < 2021
```
```
WHERE          = filter condition
admission_year = column we are checking
<              = less than operator
2021           = the value we compare to

This means:
Show only rows where
admission_year is LESS THAN 2021

So it shows:
2019 students ✓  (2019 < 2021)
2020 students ✓  (2020 < 2021)
2021 students ✗  (2021 is NOT < 2021)
2022 students ✗  (2022 is NOT < 2021)
```

---

# All variations

```sql
-- ==========================================
-- VARIATION 1: Admitted before 2021
-- Does NOT include 2021
-- ==========================================
SELECT *
FROM students
WHERE admission_year < 2021;

-- ==========================================
-- VARIATION 2: Admitted in 2021 or before
-- Includes 2021
-- ==========================================
SELECT *
FROM students
WHERE admission_year <= 2021;

-- ==========================================
-- VARIATION 3: Only specific columns
-- ==========================================
SELECT
    id,
    name,
    admission_year,
    roll_number,
    city
FROM students
WHERE admission_year < 2021;

-- ==========================================
-- VARIATION 4: Before 2021 + sorted by year
-- ==========================================
SELECT
    name,
    admission_year,
    roll_number
FROM students
WHERE admission_year < 2021
ORDER BY admission_year ASC;

-- ==========================================
-- VARIATION 5: Before 2021 + only active
-- ==========================================
SELECT
    name,
    admission_year,
    is_active
FROM students
WHERE admission_year < 2021
AND is_active = TRUE;

-- ==========================================
-- VARIATION 6: Before 2021 with count
-- ==========================================
SELECT
    admission_year,
    COUNT(*) AS total_students
FROM students
WHERE admission_year < 2021
GROUP BY admission_year
ORDER BY admission_year;

-- ==========================================
-- VARIATION 7: Using BETWEEN for a range
-- ==========================================
SELECT name, admission_year
FROM students
WHERE admission_year BETWEEN 2019 AND 2020;

-- ==========================================
-- VARIATION 8: Using date column
-- If you want before a specific date
-- ==========================================
SELECT name, date_of_birth
FROM students
WHERE date_of_birth < '2002-01-01';
```

---

# Comparison operators explained

```sql
-- All operators you can use in WHERE

-- Equal to
WHERE admission_year = 2021

-- NOT equal to
WHERE admission_year != 2021
WHERE admission_year <> 2021   -- same thing

-- Less than
WHERE admission_year < 2021

-- Less than or equal
WHERE admission_year <= 2021

-- Greater than
WHERE admission_year > 2021

-- Greater than or equal
WHERE admission_year >= 2021

-- Between two values (inclusive)
WHERE admission_year BETWEEN 2019 AND 2021

-- In a list
WHERE admission_year IN (2019, 2020)

-- Not in a list
WHERE admission_year NOT IN (2021, 2022)
```

# Practice questions on same concept

```sql
-- Try these yourself:

-- 1. Students admitted after 2020
SELECT * FROM students
WHERE admission_year > 2020;

-- 2. Students admitted in exactly 2020
SELECT * FROM students
WHERE admission_year = 2020;

-- 3. Students admitted between 2019 and 2020
SELECT * FROM students
WHERE admission_year BETWEEN 2019 AND 2020;

-- 4. Count students admitted before 2021
SELECT COUNT(*) AS total
FROM students
WHERE admission_year < 2021;

-- 5. Students admitted before 2021
-- from Mumbai only
SELECT name, city, admission_year
FROM students
WHERE admission_year < 2021
AND city = 'Mumbai';
```

---

# Quick revision

```
< 2021   = strictly before 2021 (not including 2021)
<= 2021  = 2021 and before (including 2021)
> 2021   = strictly after 2021
>= 2021  = 2021 and after
= 2021   = exactly 2021
!= 2021  = everything except 2021
```

# Quick revision before assignments

```sql
COUNT(*)     → count all rows
COUNT(col)   → count non-null values
SUM(col)     → add all values
AVG(col)     → average of values
MAX(col)     → highest value
MIN(col)     → lowest value

GROUP BY     → group rows together
HAVING       → filter groups (like WHERE for groups)

ORDER:
SELECT → FROM → WHERE → GROUP BY → HAVING → ORDER BY
```

---

## Q1) Count total number of students in the school

```
HINT:
- Use COUNT(*)
- No GROUP BY needed
- Just one number as answer
```

```sql
SELECT COUNT(*) AS total_students
FROM students;

-- OUTPUT:

-- 25
```

```
- FROM teachers table
- No GROUP BY needed
```

```sql
SELECT COUNT(*) AS total_teachers
FROM teachers;

-- OUTPUT:
```

## Q3) Find the highest salary among all teachers

```
- Use MAX()
- FROM teachers table
- salary column
```

```sql
-- YOUR ANSWER HERE:

SELECT MAX(salary) AS highest_salary
FROM teachers;
```

```
- FROM teachers table
```

```sql
FROM teachers;
```

## Q5) Find the average salary of all teachers

```
HINT:

- Use ROUND() to limit decimal places
- ROUND(AVG(salary), 2)
```

```sql
-- YOUR ANSWER HERE:




-- SOLUTION:
SELECT ROUND(AVG(salary), 2) AS avg_salary
FROM teachers;

-- OUTPUT:
-- avg_salary
-- 65133.33
```

## Q8) Find highest marks scored in results table

```
HINT:
- Use MAX()
- FROM results table
- marks column
```

```sql
-- YOUR ANSWER HERE:




-- SOLUTION:
SELECT
    MAX(marks) AS highest_marks,
    MIN(marks) AS lowest_marks,
    ROUND(AVG(marks), 2) AS avg_marks
FROM results;

-- OUTPUT:
-- highest_marks | lowest_marks | avg_marks
-- 94.00         | 25.00        | 75.23
```

# MEDIUM LEVEL

---

## Q9) Count number of students in each department

```
HINT:
- JOIN students with departments
- GROUP BY department name
- Use COUNT(*)
- ORDER BY count descending
```

```sql
-- YOUR ANSWER HERE:




-- SOLUTION:
SELECT
    d.name            AS department,
    COUNT(s.id)       AS total_students
FROM students s
JOIN departments d
    ON s.department_id = d.id
GROUP BY d.name
ORDER BY total_students DESC;

-- OUTPUT:
-- department          | total_students
-- Computer Science    | 5
-- Mathematics         | 4
-- Physics             | 3
-- ...
```

---

## Q10) Find average, max, min salary per department

```
HINT:
- JOIN teachers with departments
- GROUP BY department name
- Use AVG, MAX, MIN together
- Use ROUND for AVG
```

```sql
-- YOUR ANSWER HERE:




-- SOLUTION:
SELECT
    d.name                        AS department,
    COUNT(t.id)                   AS total_teachers,
    ROUND(AVG(t.salary), 2)       AS avg_salary,
    MAX(t.salary)                 AS max_salary,
    MIN(t.salary)                 AS min_salary
FROM teachers t
JOIN departments d
    ON t.department_id = d.id
GROUP BY d.name
ORDER BY avg_salary DESC;

-- OUTPUT:
-- department       | total | avg_salary | max    | min
-- Computer Science | 2     | 77500.00   | 80000  | 75000
-- Biology          | 1     | 72000.00   | 72000  | 72000
-- ...
```

---

## Q11) Count enrollments per course

```
HINT:
- JOIN enrollments with courses
- GROUP BY course title
- COUNT enrollments
- ORDER BY count descending
```

```sql
-- YOUR ANSWER HERE:




-- SOLUTION:
SELECT
    c.title           AS course,
    COUNT(e.id)       AS total_enrollments
FROM courses c
LEFT JOIN enrollments e
    ON c.id = e.course_id
GROUP BY c.title
ORDER BY total_enrollments DESC;

-- OUTPUT:
-- course                  | total_enrollments
-- Intro to Programming    | 5
-- Calculus                | 4
-- ...
```

---

## Q12) Find total and average marks per student

```
HINT:
- JOIN results with students
- GROUP BY student name
- Use SUM and AVG
- ORDER BY total marks descending
```

```sql
-- YOUR ANSWER HERE:




-- SOLUTION:
SELECT
    s.name                        AS student,
    COUNT(r.id)                   AS exams_taken,
    SUM(r.marks)                  AS total_marks,
    ROUND(AVG(r.marks), 2)        AS avg_marks,
    MAX(r.marks)                  AS best_mark,
    MIN(r.marks)                  AS worst_mark
FROM students s
JOIN results r
    ON s.id = r.student_id
GROUP BY s.id, s.name
ORDER BY total_marks DESC;

-- OUTPUT:
-- student     | exams | total | avg   | best | worst
-- Aman Singh  | 6     | 380   | 63.33 | 92   | 38
-- ...
```

---

## Q13) Count students by gender per department

```
HINT:
- JOIN students with departments
- GROUP BY department AND gender
- Both columns in GROUP BY
- ORDER BY department then gender
```

```sql
-- YOUR ANSWER HERE:




-- SOLUTION:
SELECT
    d.name        AS department,
    s.gender,
    COUNT(s.id)   AS count
FROM students s
JOIN departments d
    ON s.department_id = d.id
GROUP BY d.name, s.gender
ORDER BY d.name, s.gender;

-- OUTPUT:
-- department       | gender | count
-- Biology          | Female | 2
-- Chemistry        | Female | 1
-- Chemistry        | Male   | 1
-- Computer Science | Male   | 4
-- ...
```

---

## Q14) Find total fees per student with their name

```
HINT:
- JOIN fees with students
- GROUP BY student name
- SUM the amount
- Only paid fees
- ORDER BY total DESC
```

```sql
-- YOUR ANSWER HERE:




-- SOLUTION:
SELECT
    s.name              AS student,
    COUNT(f.id)         AS payment_count,
    SUM(f.amount)       AS total_paid
FROM students s
JOIN fees f
    ON s.id = f.student_id
WHERE f.status = 'paid'
GROUP BY s.id, s.name
ORDER BY total_paid DESC;

-- OUTPUT:
-- student     | payment_count | total_paid
-- Aman Singh  | 3             | 105000.00
-- Sara Khan   | 2             | 100000.00
-- ...
```

---

## Q15) Find average course fee per department

```
HINT:
- JOIN courses with departments
- GROUP BY department name
- AVG of fee column
- ROUND to 2 decimal places
```

```sql
-- YOUR ANSWER HERE:




-- SOLUTION:
SELECT
    d.name                      AS department,
    COUNT(c.id)                 AS total_courses,
    ROUND(AVG(c.fee), 2)        AS avg_course_fee,
    MAX(c.fee)                  AS max_fee,
    MIN(c.fee)                  AS min_fee
FROM courses c
JOIN departments d
    ON c.department_id = d.id
GROUP BY d.name
ORDER BY avg_course_fee DESC;

-- OUTPUT:
-- department       | courses | avg_fee | max   | min
-- Computer Science | 5       | 6100.00 | 8000  | 5000
-- Biology          | 1       | 5000.00 | 5000  | 5000
-- ...
```

# HARD LEVEL

---

## Q16) Find departments where average teacher salary is above 65000

```
HINT:
- JOIN teachers with departments
- GROUP BY department name
- Use HAVING (not WHERE) to filter groups
- HAVING AVG(salary) > 65000
```

```sql
-- YOUR ANSWER HERE:




-- SOLUTION:
SELECT
    d.name                   AS department,
    COUNT(t.id)              AS teachers,
    ROUND(AVG(t.salary), 2)  AS avg_salary
FROM teachers t
JOIN departments d
    ON t.department_id = d.id
GROUP BY d.name
HAVING AVG(t.salary) > 65000
ORDER BY avg_salary DESC;

-- OUTPUT:
-- department       | teachers | avg_salary
-- Computer Science | 2        | 77500.00
-- Biology          | 1        | 72000.00
-- Physics          | 2        | 68000.00
```

---

## Q17) Find courses with more than 2 enrollments

```
HINT:
- JOIN courses with enrollments
- GROUP BY course
- HAVING COUNT > 2
- Show course name and enrollment count
```

```sql
-- YOUR ANSWER HERE:




-- SOLUTION:
SELECT
    c.title             AS course,
    COUNT(e.id)         AS enrollments
FROM courses c
JOIN enrollments e
    ON c.id = e.course_id
GROUP BY c.id, c.title
HAVING COUNT(e.id) > 2
ORDER BY enrollments DESC;

-- OUTPUT:
-- course                 | enrollments
-- Intro to Programming   | 5
-- Calculus               | 4
-- Data Structures        | 3
-- ...
```

## Q18) Find each student's attendance percentage per course

```
HINT:
- FROM attendance table
- GROUP BY student_id AND course_id
- COUNT total classes
- COUNT present classes
- FORMULA: (present/total) * 100
- Use SUM(CASE WHEN status='present' THEN 1 ELSE 0 END)
```

```sql
-- YOUR ANSWER HERE:




-- SOLUTION:
SELECT
    s.name          AS student,
    c.title         AS course,
    COUNT(a.id)     AS total_classes,
    SUM(
        CASE WHEN a.status = 'present'
        THEN 1 ELSE 0 END
    )               AS present_count,
    ROUND(
        SUM(CASE WHEN a.status = 'present'
            THEN 1 ELSE 0 END
        ) * 100.0 / COUNT(a.id),
    2)              AS attendance_percentage
FROM attendance a
JOIN students s ON a.student_id = s.id
JOIN courses c  ON a.course_id = c.id
GROUP BY s.id, s.name, c.id, c.title
ORDER BY attendance_percentage DESC;

-- OUTPUT:
-- student    | course              | total | present | percentage
-- Raj Patel  | Intro Programming   | 2     | 2       | 100.00
-- Aman Singh | Intro Programming   | 7     | 5       | 71.43
-- ...
```

---

## Q19) Find top department by total student marks

```
HINT:
- JOIN results → students → departments
- GROUP BY department
- SUM all marks per department
- ORDER BY total marks DESC
- LIMIT 1 for top department
```

```sql
-- YOUR ANSWER HERE:




-- SOLUTION:
SELECT
    d.name              AS department,
    COUNT(DISTINCT s.id) AS students_appeared,
    COUNT(r.id)          AS total_exams,
    SUM(r.marks)         AS total_marks,
    ROUND(AVG(r.marks), 2) AS avg_marks
FROM results r
JOIN students s     ON r.student_id = s.id
JOIN departments d  ON s.department_id = d.id
GROUP BY d.id, d.name
ORDER BY total_marks DESC
LIMIT 1;

-- OUTPUT:
-- department       | students | exams | total_marks | avg
-- Computer Science | 5        | 12    | 870.00      | 72.50
```

## Q20) Find months with highest fee collection

```
HINT:
- FROM fees table
- WHERE status = 'paid'
- GROUP BY YEAR and MONTH of paid_date
- SUM amount
- Use YEAR(paid_date) and MONTH(paid_date)
- ORDER BY total DESC
```

```sql
-- YOUR ANSWER HERE:




-- SOLUTION:
SELECT
    YEAR(paid_date)     AS year,
    MONTH(paid_date)    AS month,
    COUNT(*)            AS payments,
    SUM(amount)         AS total_collected
FROM fees
WHERE status = 'paid'
GROUP BY
    YEAR(paid_date),
    MONTH(paid_date)
ORDER BY total_collected DESC;

-- OUTPUT:
-- year | month | payments | total_collected
-- 2019 | 7     | 6        | 300000.00
-- 2020 | 7     | 8        | 400000.00
-- ...
```

## Q21) Find students who have taken more than 3 exams and their average marks

```
HINT:
- JOIN results with students
- GROUP BY student
- HAVING COUNT(exams) > 3
- Show name, exam count, avg marks
```

```sql
-- YOUR ANSWER HERE:




-- SOLUTION:
SELECT
    s.name                   AS student,
    COUNT(r.id)              AS exams_taken,
    ROUND(AVG(r.marks), 2)  AS avg_marks,
    SUM(r.marks)             AS total_marks
FROM students s
JOIN results r
    ON s.id = r.student_id
GROUP BY s.id, s.name
HAVING COUNT(r.id) > 3
ORDER BY avg_marks DESC;

-- OUTPUT:
-- student    | exams | avg_marks | total
-- Aman Singh | 6     | 63.33     | 380
-- ...
```

## Q22) Find departments where total budget is above average budget

```
HINT:
- GROUP BY not needed here
- Use subquery inside WHERE
- WHERE budget > (SELECT AVG(budget) FROM departments)
- This combines aggregates with subquery
```

```sql
-- YOUR ANSWER HERE:




-- SOLUTION:
SELECT
    name,
    budget,
    ROUND(
        budget - (SELECT AVG(budget) FROM departments),
    2) AS above_avg_by
FROM departments
WHERE budget > (
    SELECT AVG(budget)
    FROM departments
)
ORDER BY budget DESC;

-- OUTPUT:
-- name             | budget    | above_avg_by
-- Computer Science | 500000.00 | 209000.00
-- Biology          | 380000.00 | 89000.00
-- Physics          | 350000.00 | 59000.00
```

---

## Q23) HARDEST: Complete department report

```
Find for each department:
- department name
- total teachers
- total students
- avg teacher salary
- total courses offered
- avg course fee
Only show departments that have
both teachers AND students
Order by total students descending

HINT:
- Need multiple JOINs
- JOIN departments with teachers, students, courses
- GROUP BY department
- HAVING to filter departments with both
- Use COUNT(DISTINCT) to avoid duplicates
```

```sql
-- YOUR ANSWER HERE:




-- SOLUTION:
SELECT
    d.name                          AS department,
    COUNT(DISTINCT t.id)            AS total_teachers,
    COUNT(DISTINCT s.id)            AS total_students,
    ROUND(AVG(DISTINCT t.salary), 2) AS avg_teacher_salary,
    COUNT(DISTINCT c.id)            AS total_courses,
    ROUND(AVG(DISTINCT c.fee), 2)   AS avg_course_fee
FROM departments d
LEFT JOIN teachers t    ON d.id = t.department_id
LEFT JOIN students s    ON d.id = s.department_id
LEFT JOIN courses c     ON d.id = c.department_id
GROUP BY d.id, d.name
HAVING
    COUNT(DISTINCT t.id) > 0
AND COUNT(DISTINCT s.id) > 0
ORDER BY total_students DESC;

-- OUTPUT:
-- dept             | teachers | students | avg_sal  | courses | avg_fee
-- Computer Science | 2        | 5        | 77500.00 | 5       | 6100.00
-- Mathematics      | 2        | 4        | 66000.00 | 3       | 4166.67
-- Physics          | 2        | 3        | 68000.00 | 2       | 4500.00
-- ...
```

```
  Q22 → aggregate with subquery
  Q23 → full department report
```

---

# Key rules to remember

```sql
-- RULE 1: WHERE filters ROWS
--         HAVING filters GROUPS

-- RULE 2: Every column in SELECT
--         must be in GROUP BY
--         OR inside aggregate function

-- RULE 3: ORDER of clauses
SELECT
FROM
WHERE      ← filters rows FIRST
GROUP BY   ← then groups
HAVING     ← then filters groups
ORDER BY   ← then sorts

-- RULE 4: HAVING can use
--         aggregate functions
--         WHERE cannot

-- RULE 5: Use COUNT(DISTINCT col)
--         to count unique values only
```

4. **Real interview questions using this database**

TOPIC 3: GROUP BY and Aggregate Functions give assigment from easy to hard give hint also give basic example also

*System: User retried message*

## Copilot

Here's a **complete GROUP BY and Aggregate Functions guide** from easy to hard.

---

# First understand the basics

---

## What are Aggregate Functions?

```
Normal function  → works on ONE row
Aggregate function → works on MANY rows
                     gives ONE result
```

---

## Simple real life example

```
Your class has 5 students with marks:
80, 90, 70, 85, 75

COUNT  → how many students?  = 5
SUM    → total marks?        = 400
AVG    → average marks?      = 80
MAX    → highest marks?      = 90
MIN    → lowest marks?       = 70
```

---

## All aggregate functions

```sql
-- ==========================================
-- COUNT - how many rows
-- ==========================================
SELECT COUNT(*)           FROM students;  -- all rows
SELECT COUNT(email)       FROM students;  -- non-null only
SELECT COUNT(DISTINCT city) FROM students; -- unique cities

-- ==========================================
-- SUM - add up values
-- ==========================================
SELECT SUM(salary)  FROM teachers;
SELECT SUM(amount)  FROM fees;
SELECT SUM(marks)   FROM results;

-- ==========================================
-- AVG - average value
-- ==========================================
SELECT AVG(salary)  FROM teachers;
SELECT AVG(marks)   FROM results;
SELECT AVG(fee)     FROM courses;

-- ==========================================
-- MAX - highest value
-- ==========================================
SELECT MAX(salary)  FROM teachers;
SELECT MAX(marks)   FROM results;
SELECT MAX(amount)  FROM fees;

-- ==========================================
-- MIN - lowest value
-- ==========================================
SELECT MIN(salary)  FROM teachers;
SELECT MIN(marks)   FROM results;
SELECT MIN(fee)     FROM courses;

-- ==========================================
-- ALL TOGETHER
-- ==========================================
SELECT
    COUNT(*)          AS total_teachers,
    SUM(salary)       AS total_salary,
    ROUND(AVG(salary),2) AS avg_salary,
    MAX(salary)       AS highest_salary,
    MIN(salary)       AS lowest_salary
FROM teachers;
```

---

## What is GROUP BY?

```
Without GROUP BY:
  Aggregate works on WHOLE table

With GROUP BY:
  Aggregate works on EACH GROUP separately

Think of it like:
  Separate the data into groups
  Then apply aggregate to each group
```

---

## Simple GROUP BY example

```sql
-- WITHOUT GROUP BY
-- One result for whole table
SELECT AVG(salary) FROM teachers;
-- Result: 65000 (overall average)

-- WITH GROUP BY
-- One result PER DEPARTMENT
SELECT
    department_id,
    AVG(salary) AS avg_salary
FROM teachers
GROUP BY department_id;
-- Result:
-- dept 1 → 77500
-- dept 2 → 66000
-- dept 3 → 68000
```

---

## GROUP BY rules - VERY IMPORTANT

```sql
-- RULE 1:
-- Every column in SELECT must be either:
-- a) In GROUP BY clause
-- b) Inside an aggregate function

-- WRONG ❌
SELECT name, department_id, AVG(salary)
FROM teachers
GROUP BY department_id;
-- name is not in GROUP BY and not aggregated!

-- CORRECT ✓
SELECT department_id, AVG(salary)
FROM teachers
GROUP BY department_id;

-- CORRECT ✓
SELECT department_id, name, AVG(salary)
FROM teachers
GROUP BY department_id, name;
```

---

## What is HAVING?

```
WHERE  → filters ROWS   (before grouping)
HAVING → filters GROUPS (after grouping)

You CANNOT use aggregate in WHERE
You CAN use aggregate in HAVING
```

---

## HAVING example

```sql
-- WHERE filters individual rows
SELECT department_id, AVG(salary)
FROM teachers
WHERE is_active = TRUE        -- filter rows first
GROUP BY department_id
HAVING AVG(salary) > 65000;   -- then filter groups

-- WRONG ❌ - cannot use aggregate in WHERE
SELECT department_id, AVG(salary)
FROM teachers
WHERE AVG(salary) > 65000    -- ERROR!
GROUP BY department_id;
```

---

## Order of SQL clauses

```sql
SELECT   →  what to show
FROM     →  which table
WHERE    →  filter rows
GROUP BY →  group the rows
HAVING   →  filter groups
ORDER BY →  sort results
LIMIT    →  restrict rows

-- Always in this order!
SELECT department_id, COUNT(*), AVG(salary)
FROM teachers
WHERE is_active = TRUE
GROUP BY department_id
HAVING COUNT(*) > 1
ORDER BY AVG(salary) DESC
LIMIT 5;
```

---

# Basic Examples on our database

```sql
-- ==========================================
-- EXAMPLE 1: Count students per department
-- ==========================================
SELECT
    d.name       AS department,
    COUNT(s.id)  AS total_students
FROM students s
JOIN departments d
    ON s.department_id = d.id
GROUP BY d.name
ORDER BY total_students DESC;

/*
Result:
department        | total_students
------------------|---------------
Computer Science  | 5
Mathematics       | 4
Physics           | 3
...
*/

-- ==========================================
-- EXAMPLE 2: Avg salary per department
-- ==========================================
SELECT
    d.name              AS department,
    COUNT(t.id)         AS teachers,
    ROUND(AVG(t.salary),2) AS avg_salary,
    MAX(t.salary)       AS max_salary,
    MIN(t.salary)       AS min_salary
FROM teachers t
JOIN departments d
    ON t.department_id = d.id
GROUP BY d.name
ORDER BY avg_salary DESC;

-- ==========================================
-- EXAMPLE 3: HAVING - dept with >2 teachers
-- ==========================================
SELECT
    d.name,
    COUNT(t.id) AS teacher_count
FROM teachers t
JOIN departments d
    ON t.department_id = d.id
GROUP BY d.name
HAVING COUNT(t.id) > 2;

-- ==========================================
-- EXAMPLE 4: Total fees per student
-- ==========================================
SELECT
    s.name,
    COUNT(f.id)    AS payments,
    SUM(f.amount)  AS total_paid
FROM fees f
JOIN students s
    ON f.student_id = s.id
WHERE f.status = 'paid'
GROUP BY s.name
ORDER BY total_paid DESC;

-- ==========================================
-- EXAMPLE 5: Avg marks per exam type
-- ==========================================
SELECT
    e.exam_type,
    COUNT(r.id)          AS total_results,
    ROUND(AVG(r.marks),2) AS avg_marks,
    MAX(r.marks)          AS highest,
    MIN(r.marks)          AS lowest
FROM results r
JOIN exams e
    ON r.exam_id = e.id
GROUP BY e.exam_type;
```

# ASSIGNMENTS - Easy to Hard

---

# LEVEL 1 - EASY 🟢

---

## Assignment 1 - Easy

```sql
-- HINT: Use COUNT(*) with no GROUP BY
-- Just count all rows in table

-- Q1: Count total number of students
-- HINT: SELECT COUNT(*) FROM ...

-- YOUR ANSWER:
SELECT COUNT(*) AS total_students
FROM students;
```

---

## Assignment 2 - Easy

```sql
-- HINT: Use SUM() on salary column
-- No GROUP BY needed

-- Q2: Find total salary paid to all teachers
-- HINT: SELECT SUM(column) FROM ...

-- YOUR ANSWER:
SELECT SUM(salary) AS total_salary_paid
FROM teachers;
```

---

## Assignment 3 - Easy

```sql
-- HINT: Use AVG() on marks column

-- Q3: Find average marks in results table
-- HINT: SELECT AVG(column) FROM ...

-- YOUR ANSWER:
SELECT ROUND(AVG(marks), 2) AS average_marks
FROM results;
```

---

## Assignment 4 - Easy

```sql
-- HINT: Use MAX() and MIN() together

-- Q4: Find highest and lowest course fee
-- HINT: SELECT MAX(col), MIN(col) FROM ...

-- YOUR ANSWER:
SELECT
    MAX(fee) AS highest_fee,
    MIN(fee) AS lowest_fee
FROM courses;
```

---

## Assignment 5 - Easy

```sql
-- HINT: Use GROUP BY on gender column
-- Use COUNT(*) to count each group

-- Q5: Count students by gender
-- HINT: GROUP BY gender

-- YOUR ANSWER:
SELECT
    gender,
    COUNT(*) AS total
FROM students
GROUP BY gender;
```
